He who stops at what we have explained of the aspects knows the reality and does not shy away from affirming that when faced with the intimidation of the Mu'tazila, and he makes clear to them that he says what they say, and all rational people are compelled to affirm it. Then all this from us proceeded with the opponents in a lenient manner. So if we were to oppose them and say: This from you is a specification of some points of disagreement. If your deterrent from saying that actions are created by God is the impossibility of attributing evil to God Almighty, then what is your deterrent regarding attributing acts of obedience to God Almighty, which are wisdom and good things? So say they are created due to the absence of this deterrent in them; otherwise your obstinacy becomes evident. Moreover, according to you, all acts of obedience are by the will of God Almighty, and God Almighty says: 'Doer of what He wills'. So acts of obedience are done by Him because they are willed by Him, and He says: 'Doer of what He wills'. So either they affirm that acts of obedience are created by Him, or they say they are not by His will, thus invalidating their doctrine, or they say that although they are willed by Him, He does not do them, thus denying the text and rejecting it—and therein lies its trouble. Then we say: There is no doubt that the goodness of acts of obedience is superior to the goodness of bodies, because their goodness is intellectual and it does not vary with the variation of senses. However, according to the Mu'tazila, there is no basis for saying that bodies are good, because they deny the ugliness of what is ugly among them, even if that is known by sense. So likewise they are compelled to deny sense, since it is only known by what ugliness is known by, and there is no reality to that knowledge, so likewise to this. The goodness of acts of obedience is intellectual, and it is something that does not vary with the variation of intellects nor changes from state to state. The superiority of agents is by the superiority of actions. So it must be that every obedient servant is better than God Almighty, and better in action, and higher in rank, because the rank of his action is above the action of God Almighty, and the rank of His action—glorified and exalted—is lower than the actions of servants. And the servant has in his power of good things what is not similar to or near that in the power of God Almighty. Saying that is a saying whose content is not hidden. So the power of God Almighty is Iblis, apes, filth, stench, decrepitude, blindness, paralysis, diseases, death, and calamities. The power of the servant is fasting, prayer, pilgrimage, lending, believing, affirmation of divine unity, bestowing upon the needy, kindness to them, fulfilling covenants, rendering trusts, generosity, magnanimity, and other virtues that fall under the power of servants. Each of the agents is apportioned according to what is in his power, and he departs with what he created. The servant obtains the best portion and the greatest lot, and the power of the Eternal falls short of that. Because the Mu'tazila know of this necessary consequence, they turn away from the side of good things completely and cling to ugly things, hoping perhaps to market their falsehood among weak Muslims. May God Almighty protect us from that through His generosity and bounty, and He is the possessor of great bounty.
Source pages p. 937

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila concerning the creation of human actions. The author first notes that anyone who understands the previous arguments will not be intimidated by the Mu'tazila's blustering. He then presents a softening concession (on the path of leniency) before launching a more direct attack. The argument is: if the Mu'tazila deny that God creates all actions because creating evil is impossible for God, then they must affirm that God creates acts of obedience (since those are good, and no such impossibility exists). If they refuse, they are being obstinate. Moreover, they already believe that all acts of obedience are by God's will (Quran 11:107), so they must admit either that God creates them (contradicting their denial), or that they are not by His will (invalidating their own doctrine), or that God wills them but does not create them (rejecting the plain sense of the Quran). The author then goes further: the goodness of acts of obedience is intellectual and superior to the sensory goodness of bodies, so it is even more necessary that God create them. The Mu'tazila's denial leads to absurd conclusions: if actions are not created by God, then human actions are better than God's actions, making the obedient servant superior to God in action and rank. God's power would then be limited to ugly, base things (like Iblis, disease, etc.) while human power encompasses virtues like prayer, charity, and belief. This is manifestly false. The Mu'tazila, aware of this forced consequence, avoid discussing good actions and instead cling to the problem of evil, hoping to deceive the weak-minded. The passage concludes with a prayer for protection.

Key Terms
حسن
عقلي
تفاضل الفاعلين
مقدور
إلزام
طاعات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila have a consistent position that God cannot create any human action.
Correction: The author shows that their position leads to the absurdity that human actions are better than God's and God's power is limited to evil, which is inconsistent with Islamic monotheism.

Checkpoint
What is the main consequence the author draws if the Mu'tazila deny that God creates acts of obedience?
A) Acts of obedience become impossible for humans.
B) The obedient servant would be better than God in action and rank.
C) The Quran becomes contradictory.
D) God's will becomes irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Denying that God creates good actions leads to the blasphemous conclusion that humans are superior to God and that God's power is limited to evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage ends with a prayer: 'May God Almighty protect us from that.' Where in your life are you subtly assuming that your good deeds come from your own independent power rather than God's creation? How would acknowledging God as the sole creator of every good action change your humility and gratitude today?
?
If the Mu'tazila were wrong in limiting God's power to avoid the problem of evil, where might you be tempted to limit God's power in your own life to make sense of suffering? Are you willing to submit to a God whose power includes both good and evil, even if you cannot fully comprehend His wisdom?
?
Consider the list of virtues the author attributes to the servant's power: prayer, charity, truthfulness, generosity. Do you ever pride yourself on these as your personal achievements? What would it mean for you to truly believe that every single one of them is created by God and not by yourself? Would you still strive just as hard?

Divine Decree
beginner

The Objection and the Divine Analogy
Nasafi presents the objection and offers the divine analogy as a first reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the analogy of God's own knowledge to refute the claim that divine foreknowledge compels human actions.

Core Idea
God's knowledge does not compel Him, so it cannot compel us.

This lesson tackles a classic objection: if God creates all human acts, how can we be free? Nasafi responds by pointing to God's own relationship with His knowledge. Just as God's knowledge of His own choices does not force Him, so His knowledge of our choices does not force us. This analogy preserves both divine foreknowledge and human free will.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the first part of the passage where the objector claims that if God creates all acts, the slave cannot escape them and therefore cannot be free.
Notice the phrasing: 'فإن زعموا أن العبد لا يمكنه الخروج عما خلقه فكيف يكون مختارا؟'
2
Follow Nasafi's initial reply
Nasafi first shifts the focus from creation to knowledge: the slave cannot exit God's knowledge, but neither can God exit His own knowledge. Yet God is not compelled.
This sets up the analogy: God's knowledge does not force Him, so why would it force us?
3
Understand the core analogy
Nasafi argues that God's knowledge of His own act includes that He acts by choice. If that knowledge compelled Him, it would contradict itself—He would both know He chooses and be forced. That is impossible.
Thus, knowledge does not entail compulsion.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this initial argument undermines the objector's assumption. The slave's situation is analogous: God knows the slave acts by choice, so His knowledge cannot compel the slave.
The full reasoning is developed further in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباري مضطرًا في تخليقه، فإن زعموا أن العبد لا يمكنه الخروج عما خلقه فكيف يكون مختارا؟ قلنا : العبد لا يمكنه الخروج عن معلوم الباري، فكيف يكون مختارا والباري لا يخرج أيضا عن معلومه فكيف يكون مختارا؟ وجاء من هذا أنه تعالى لما لم يكن في فعله مضطرًا وإن كان لا يخرج عن معلومه لأنه وإن كان لا يخرج عن معلومه فمعلومه أنه يفعل ما يفعل باختياره فلو صار بعلمه مضطرًا لانقلب جاهلاً حيث علم أنه يفعل ما يفعل باختياره، فلم يفعل بل فعل مضطرًا فكذا عبده لا يصير مضطرًا بعلمه وإن كان لا يخرج عن معلومه لأن معلومه تعالى أن العبد يفعل ما يفعل باختياره غير مضطر ، فلو صار مضطرًا لانقلب علم الباري جهلاً وذلك محال، فبقى العبد مختارا فكذا لما خلق بعلمه الاختياري يبقى مختاراً؛ إذ لو انقلب بذلك مضطرًا لم يحصل ما خلق على ما خلق بل على غير ما خلق وهو أمارة اضطراره، فكان القول بما قالوا موجبا كون فعل الله تعالى بالاضطرار وهذا محال ، وإذا لم يصر العبد بتخليق فعله مضطرًا في فعله كما لم يصر بتخليق السماوات والأرض فكان خلق كفره وخلق السماوات والأرضين والجبال والبحار سواء من أن لا يوجب اضطرار العبد، والله الموفق.
Is God compelled in His creation? For if they claim that the slave cannot exit what He created for him, then how is he free? We said: The slave cannot exit what God knows, so how is he free while God also does not exit what He knows, so how is He free? And it follows from this that He, exalted, since He is not compelled in His act even though He does not exit what He knows – because even though He does not exit what He knows, what He knows is that He does what He does by His choice – so if He became compelled by His knowledge, He would turn ignorant, since He knows that He does what He does by choice, and yet He did not do it but did it compelled. Likewise, His slave does not become compelled by His knowledge even though he does not exit what He knows, because what God knows is that the slave does what he does by his choice, not compelled. So if he became compelled, God’s knowledge would turn to ignorance, and that is impossible. Thus the slave remains free. Similarly, since He created him with His knowledge, he remains free; for if he turned compelled by that, then what He created would not occur as He created it, but contrary to how He created it, and that is a sign of compulsion. So the statement they made necessitates that God’s act is by compulsion, and that is impossible. And when the slave does not become compelled in his act by God’s creation of his act, just as he does not become compelled by the creation of the heavens and the earth, then the creation of his disbelief and the creation of the heavens and the earths and the mountains and the seas are equal in that they do not necessitate the slave’s compulsion. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 942

Commentary
This is an argument-and-rebuttal passage addressing a deterministic objection. The objector claims: if God creates all human acts, then the slave cannot exit God's creation, so he cannot be free. Nasafi replies by drawing an analogy with God himself: God cannot exit His own knowledge, yet He is not compelled because His knowledge includes that He acts by choice. If God became compelled by His knowledge, His knowledge would contradict itself (He knows He chooses, yet He is forced). Similarly, God’s knowledge of the slave’s act includes that the slave acts by choice. If the slave became compelled, God’s knowledge would turn into ignorance—impossible. Nasafi then extends the analogy: just as the creation of the heavens and earth does not compel the slave, so the creation of his act does not compel him. The argument preserves both divine foreknowledge and human free choice, rejecting the idea that God’s knowledge necessitates compulsion.

Key Terms
اختيار (choice)
اضطرار (compulsion)
معلوم (known)
علم (knowledge)
تخليق (creation of act)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God knows an action, the doer is compelled to perform it.
Correction: Nasafi shows that knowledge does not force the action; God's own knowledge of His choice does not compel Him, so neither does His knowledge of our choices compel us.

Checkpoint
What is the initial response Nasafi gives to the objection that God's creation compels the slave?
A) He denies that God creates all acts.
B) He argues that the slave cannot escape God's knowledge, just as God cannot escape His own knowledge, yet God is free.
C) He says the slave's act is not created by God.
D) He claims that God's knowledge is limited.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of His own choice does not compel Him, so His knowledge of our choice cannot compel us either.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge of your actions does not force them, then when you sin, why do you sometimes tell yourself 'it was written for me' as if that excuses you? What would it mean for your repentance if you truly accepted that you could have chosen otherwise?
?
Think of a moment you felt completely trapped—like you had no choice. How does Nasafi's argument that even God is not compelled by His own knowledge challenge your feeling of being forced? Could it be that your sense of helplessness is a lie you believe?
?
If God knows every sin you will ever commit and still holds you accountable, does that make Him unjust in your eyes? How does this passage’s distinction between knowledge and compulsion shape your answer—and what does your hesitation reveal about your heart?

Preserving Free Will Through Divine Knowledge
Nasafi applies the same reasoning to the slave and adds the creation of the universe analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's deeper argument that if divine knowledge compelled the slave, it would turn into ignorance, and the final analogy with the creation of the universe.

Core Idea
If knowledge compelled, it would become ignorance—impossible.

Building on the divine analogy, Nasafi now applies the same reasoning directly to the slave. He then extends the argument with a striking analogy: just as the creation of the heavens and earth does not compel the slave, the creation of his act does not either. This lesson completes the refutation and reinforces the consistency of divine foreknowledge with human free choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Begin with 'فكذا عبده لا يصير مضطرًا بعلمه' and continue to the end. This section applies the same logic to the slave and adds the creation analogy.
Pay attention to the conditional: 'فلو صار مضطرًا لانقلب علم الباري جهلاً'.
2
Understand the argument for the slave
Nasafi argues that God knows the slave acts by choice. If that knowledge compelled the slave, God's knowledge would be false—He would know choice but compulsion occurs. That would turn knowledge into ignorance, which is impossible.
This mirrors the earlier argument for God Himself.
3
Examine the extension to creation
Nasafi then compares the creation of the slave's act to the creation of the heavens and earth. Neither compels the slave, because they are external to his choice.
The slave remains free despite God's creation of his act.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Nasafi concludes that the objector's claim would imply God acts by compulsion, which is absurd. Therefore, the slave's free will is preserved.
Note the final phrase: 'والله الموفق'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباري مضطرًا في تخليقه، فإن زعموا أن العبد لا يمكنه الخروج عما خلقه فكيف يكون مختارا؟ قلنا : العبد لا يمكنه الخروج عن معلوم الباري، فكيف يكون مختارا والباري لا يخرج أيضا عن معلومه فكيف يكون مختارا؟ وجاء من هذا أنه تعالى لما لم يكن في فعله مضطرًا وإن كان لا يخرج عن معلومه لأنه وإن كان لا يخرج عن معلومه فمعلومه أنه يفعل ما يفعل باختياره فلو صار بعلمه مضطرًا لانقلب جاهلاً حيث علم أنه يفعل ما يفعل باختياره، فلم يفعل بل فعل مضطرًا فكذا عبده لا يصير مضطرًا بعلمه وإن كان لا يخرج عن معلومه لأن معلومه تعالى أن العبد يفعل ما يفعل باختياره غير مضطر ، فلو صار مضطرًا لانقلب علم الباري جهلاً وذلك محال، فبقى العبد مختارا فكذا لما خلق بعلمه الاختياري يبقى مختاراً؛ إذ لو انقلب بذلك مضطرًا لم يحصل ما خلق على ما خلق بل على غير ما خلق وهو أمارة اضطراره، فكان القول بما قالوا موجبا كون فعل الله تعالى بالاضطرار وهذا محال ، وإذا لم يصر العبد بتخليق فعله مضطرًا في فعله كما لم يصر بتخليق السماوات والأرض فكان خلق كفره وخلق السماوات والأرضين والجبال والبحار سواء من أن لا يوجب اضطرار العبد، والله الموفق.
Is God compelled in His creation? For if they claim that the slave cannot exit what He created for him, then how is he free? We said: The slave cannot exit what God knows, so how is he free while God also does not exit what He knows, so how is He free? And it follows from this that He, exalted, since He is not compelled in His act even though He does not exit what He knows – because even though He does not exit what He knows, what He knows is that He does what He does by His choice – so if He became compelled by His knowledge, He would turn ignorant, since He knows that He does what He does by choice, and yet He did not do it but did it compelled. Likewise, His slave does not become compelled by His knowledge even though he does not exit what He knows, because what God knows is that the slave does what he does by his choice, not compelled. So if he became compelled, God’s knowledge would turn to ignorance, and that is impossible. Thus the slave remains free. Similarly, since He created him with His knowledge, he remains free; for if he turned compelled by that, then what He created would not occur as He created it, but contrary to how He created it, and that is a sign of compulsion. So the statement they made necessitates that God’s act is by compulsion, and that is impossible. And when the slave does not become compelled in his act by God’s creation of his act, just as he does not become compelled by the creation of the heavens and the earth, then the creation of his disbelief and the creation of the heavens and the earths and the mountains and the seas are equal in that they do not necessitate the slave’s compulsion. And God is the Grantor of success.

Commentary
This is an argument-and-rebuttal passage addressing a deterministic objection. The objector claims: if God creates all human acts, then the slave cannot exit God's creation, so he cannot be free. Nasafi replies by drawing an analogy with God himself: God cannot exit His own knowledge, yet He is not compelled because His knowledge includes that He acts by choice. If God became compelled by His knowledge, His knowledge would contradict itself (He knows He chooses, yet He is forced). Similarly, God’s knowledge of the slave’s act includes that the slave acts by choice. If the slave became compelled, God’s knowledge would turn into ignorance—impossible. Nasafi then extends the analogy: just as the creation of the heavens and earth does not compel the slave, so the creation of his act does not compel him. The argument preserves both divine foreknowledge and human free choice, rejecting the idea that God’s knowledge necessitates compulsion.

اضطرار (compulsion)

Correction: Nasafi shows that creation of an act, like creation of the universe, does not remove the creature's freedom.

What does Nasafi say would happen if the slave became compelled by God's knowledge?
A) The slave would be punished justly.
B) God's knowledge would turn into ignorance.
C) The slave would still be free.

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge and creation do not compel human actions; the slave remains free because God knows he chooses freely.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you know that God's knowledge does not compel you, what excuse do you have for any sin you commit? Will you take full responsibility and repent today, without blaming destiny?
?
When you feel overwhelmed by circumstances, do you forget that God's creation of your actions does not rob you of choice? What specific choice can you make today to exercise your freedom in obedience to Allah?

Divine Decree

Refuting Abu Hashim: The Omission Dilemma and Jabriyya Consequence
Nasafi refutes Abu Hashim's coercion claim and derives a dilemma about punishment without action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's refutation of Abu Hashim's claim that the slave is compelled because he cannot avoid God's creation, and why this leads to an absurd consequence about punishment without action.

Core Idea
If doing nothing is possible, coercion is false; punishment without action is pure determinism.

In this passage, Nasafi takes on Abu Hashim's argument that the slave is inevitably coerced because he cannot escape God's creation altogether. He shows that a truly capable agent can simply remain inactive, avoiding both action and its opposite. Then he draws a startling consequence: if inaction is possible, then missing prayer without any act would not warrant punishment, yet Abu Hashim would still punish—revealing his position as worse than the Jabriyya.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of argument. Note that it begins with Abu Hashim's claim and then Nasafi's rebuttal.
Pay attention to the terms 'إكراه' (coercion) and 'خلو عن الفعل والترك' (emptiness from act and omission).
2
Identify Abu Hashim's claim and Nasafi's counter
Abu Hashim argues that the slave is compelled because he cannot exit both motion and its opposite. Nasafi counters that a fully capable agent can be empty of both—i.e., do nothing.
This is the key move: Nasafi introduces the possibility of 'doing nothing' as a way to avoid coercion.
3
Understand the prayer-time dilemma
Nasafi draws a consequence: if the slave can do nothing, then someone who lets prayer time pass without performing or abandoning it (no act) should not be punished. Yet Abu Hashim admits he would be punished, but cannot base it on an act.
This dilemma shows the inconsistency in Abu Hashim's position.
4
Recognize the Jabriyya conclusion
Nasafi declares that Abu Hashim's view is exactly the doctrine of the Jabriyya (determinists) and even more extreme, because he punishes without any act at all.
Think about why this is 'more excessive' than the Jabriyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و عورض أبو هاشم في قوله : إن العبد لما لم يمكنه الخروج عن مخلوق الله تعالى كان مضطرًا إذ العبد لا يمكنه الخروج عن الحركة وضدها، فكان مكرها على أحدهما ، فزعم أنه يمكنه الخروج عنهما ، وزعم أن القادر السليم الآلة التام القدرة يجوز خلوه عن الفعل وتركه فكان متمكنا من الخروج عنهما، فلم يكن مكرهًا على أحدهما فارتكب المحال بذلك، ولزمه أن وقت صلاة لو انقضى وهو لم يفعل شيئًا لا فعلها ولا تركها أن لا يعاقب، لأنه لم يفعل فعلاً قبيحا ومن المحال أن المأمور بالصلاة يمضي على وقت الصلاة وهو لم يصلها ولا يستحق العقاب فزعم أنه يعاقب، قيل: على ماذا يعاقب ولم يفعل فعلاً؟ فقال: يعاقب لا على فعل وجد منه، وهذا عين مذهب الجبرية: أن العبد يعاقب لا على فعل وجد منه، وهذا أربى عليهم
And Abu Hashim was rebutted in his statement: that since the slave cannot exit from what God created, he is compelled; because the slave cannot exit from motion and its opposite, so he is coerced into one of them. He (Nasafi) claimed that he can exit both of them, and he claimed that the powerful one with sound instrument and perfect power can be empty of both act and omission, so he is able to exit both, and thus he is not coerced into either. So he (Abu Hashim) committed the impossible by that. And it follows for him that if a prayer time passes and he (the slave) does nothing—neither performing it nor abandoning it—that he should not be punished, because he did not do an ugly act. And it is impossible that the one commanded to pray lets the prayer time pass without praying and does not deserve punishment. But he claimed that he is punished. It was said: for what is he punished, since he did no act? He said: he is punished not for an act that occurred from him. This is exactly the doctrine of the Jabriyya: that the slave is punished not for an act that occurred from him. And this is even more excessive than them.
Source pages p. 942

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Abu Hashim, a Mu'tazili theologian. Abu Hashim argued that because the slave cannot exit God's creation entirely—specifically, he cannot avoid both motion and its opposite (i.e., action and inaction)—he is necessarily coerced into one. Nasafi counters that a fully capable agent (perfect power, sound instrument) can remain without doing either; the slave can exit both by doing nothing. Therefore, he is not coerced into either. Then Nasafi draws a damning consequence: if Abu Hashim's view were true, then a person who lets the prayer time pass without performing it and without explicitly abandoning it (no act at all) should not be punished, because no evil act occurred. Yet Abu Hashim admits he would be punished, but cannot base it on a positive act. This, Nasafi says, is pure Jabr (determinism) — punishment without any act — and is even worse than the Jabriyya themselves, who at least admit that acts are created by God.

Key Terms
إكراه (coercion)
قادر سليم الآلة (capable with sound instrument)
خلو عن الفعل والترك (emptiness from act and omission)
جبرية (Jabriyya/Determinists)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a person does nothing, they cannot be held accountable; Nasafi shows this leads to absurdity for determinists.
Correction: Nasafi argues that the possibility of doing nothing refutes coercion, but Abu Hashim's position leads to punishing without an act, which is even more extreme than the Jabriyya.

Checkpoint
What problem does Nasafi derive from Abu Hashim's view that the slave cannot exit God's creation?
A) The slave becomes compelled to sin.
B) The slave who does nothing during prayer time would be punished without an act.
C) The slave can never be free.
D) God becomes responsible for sin.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi demonstrates that Abu Hashim's position on coercion leads to the absurdity of punishing without an act, revealing it as pure determinism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you avoid a sin by doing nothing, do you ever feel you are not responsible? This lesson shows that even 'doing nothing' is an act of omission for which you are accountable. How often do you use passivity as a shield against responsibility before Allah?
?
Abu Hashim's error was to think that compulsion comes from inability to avoid both opposites. In your life, where do you claim 'I had no choice' when in fact you had the third option of doing nothing? Are you willing to face that you are not coerced by circumstance?
?
Nasafi says punishment without act is pure determinism. Do you secretly believe that God will punish you for things you didn't actually do, just because of your fate? This lesson refutes that. How does this affect your fear of God's justice and your motivation to act righteously?

The Jabriyya Contradiction: God Compelled to Be Just?
Nasafi critiques the Jabriyya for their doctrine of compulsion leading to divine compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the internal contradiction within the Jabriyya position as exposed by Imam Nasafi: that claiming God can compel faith ultimately forces God to be compelled to justice.

Core Idea
The Jabriyya's view forces God to be compelled—contradicting His absolute sovereignty.

Imam Nasafi here exposes a devastating internal contradiction in the Jabriyya (determinist) position. They claim God can compel a person to faith by creating fear of punishment. But then, to avoid ceasing to be God, He must be just—making Him compelled to justice. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the full argument. Notice the structure: a refutation of a previous claim, an accusation of hypocrisy, and a logical unfolding.
The passage ends abruptly; the conclusion is implied.
2
Identify the Jabriyya's two-step reasoning
The Jabriyya argue that God can compel faith by creating knowledge of painful punishment. Then they admit God could do injustice, but if He did, His divinity would cease. Follow this logic.
Pay attention to the terms 'جبر' and 'مشيئة'.
3
Analyze the contradiction Nasafi points out
Nasafi shows that on their own assumptions, God is compelled to be just and truthful to avoid the great harm of losing divinity. This contradicts their claim that God is not compelled.
This is the key teaching point: the Jabriyya end up affirming compulsion for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال : أن هذا يجب على الله تعالى تعذيبه وجوبًا لو امتنع عن ذلك إذا خرج العبد من الدنيا قبل التوبة لصار سفيها وزالت ربوبيته، وقد رددنا هذا عليه في مسألة الاستطاعة ، وأعدنا ذكر هذه المسألة هاهنا ليعرف العاقل مذهبهم الذي عليه حذاقهم في زماننا هذا، ويعلم أن نسبة هؤلاء خصومهم إلى الجبر أنهم يثبتون للعبد الفعل ويعرفون الاختيار ونفي الاضطرار بالضرورة مع أن هذه مقالتهم وقاحة عظيمة وظلم ظاهر، ثم من مذهبه في تفسير مشيئة الجبر وأن الله تعالى قادر أن يجبر العبد على الإيمان بأن يخلق له علمًا يعلم بأنه لو لم يؤمن يعذب عذابًا أليما فيصير بذلك العلم مجبوراً على الإيمان، ثم هو يزعم أن الله تعالى قادر على الظلم والكذب ولو ظلم أو كذب لاستحق الذنب وزالت ربوبيته، فصار على تقدير قوله مجبوراً على العدل والصدق ؛ إذ لو لم يفعله للحقه ضرر عظيم إذ لا ضرر أعظم من زوال الألوهية وبطلان الربوبية، فكان هذا الرجل قائلاً
And he said: that it is necessarily incumbent upon God to punish him; if He refrained from that when the slave leaves the world before repentance, He would become foolish and His lordship would cease. And we have already refuted this against him in the issue of capability (al-istiṭāʿa). And we repeat this issue here so that the intelligent person may know their doctrine which their clever ones hold in our time, and know that the attribution of these opponents to determinism (jabr)—that they affirm for the slave the act and recognize choice and negation of compulsion necessarily—while this their statement is great insolence and manifest injustice. Then, from his doctrine in the interpretation of the will (mashīʾa) of compulsion is that God is able to compel the slave to faith by creating for him knowledge whereby he knows that if he does not believe, he will suffer a painful punishment, so by that knowledge he becomes compelled to faith. Then he claims that God is able to do injustice and lying, and if He did injustice or lied, He would deserve sin and His lordship would cease. So according to his assumption, He becomes compelled to justice and truth; for if He did not do them, a great harm would befall Him—and there is no greater harm than the loss of divinity and the nullification of lordship. So this man was saying...
Source pages p. 943

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the Jabriyya (determinists) and their doctrine of compulsion. Nasafi first references a previous refutation regarding divine punishment and repentance. He then exposes the hypocrisy of his opponents: they accuse others of determinism while themselves affirming human act and choice, yet their own position leads to absurdities. The main critique: the Jabriyya say God can compel a person to faith by creating knowledge of severe punishment, making that person compelled by fear. But then, since God is able to do injustice and lying, if He did them He would lose His divinity. Therefore, to avoid the great harm of ceasing to be God, He is compelled to be just and truthful. Thus the Jabriyya end up affirming compulsion even for God—a self-contradiction. Nasafi leaves the sentence unfinished, but the implication is clear: the Jabriyya are inconsistent.

Key Terms
جبر (compulsion/determinism)
مشيئة (will)
الإيمان (faith)
ظلم (injustice)
كذب (lying)
ربوبية (lordship)
ألوهية (divinity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Jabriyya only deny human free will and do not attribute compulsion to God.
Correction: Nasafi shows that their view logically forces God to be compelled to be just, contradicting divine sovereignty.

Checkpoint
What contradiction does Nasafi point out in the Jabriyya position described?
A) They say God can compel faith but then must be compelled to be just.
B) They deny human free will but affirm it for God.
C) They claim God is powerful but cannot punish.
D) They say faith is by choice but punishment is obligatory.

Key Takeaway
The Jabriyya position that God can compel faith logically leads to God being compelled to be just, contradicting divine sovereignty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's power, do you ever assume that because He can do all things, He must be bound by nothing? How does Nasafi's exposure of the Jabriyya's hidden assumption—that God must be just or lose His lordship—make you reconsider what 'Almighty' truly means? What is one area where you have assumed God's freedom is limited by your own expectations, and how will you surrender that assumption today?
?
The Jabriyya claimed God can compel faith, but Nasafi showed this forces God to be compelled. How comfortable are you with the idea that your own theology might contain hidden contradictions that make God subject to something? What is one belief you hold about God that you have never tested for consistency with His absolute sovereignty? Will you examine it honestly today?
?
If God's lordship could be nullified by actions like injustice or lying, as the Jabriyya feared, would you worship a God who was ever constrained to be good? Or is your worship based on the unforced perfection of His nature? What fears about God's character do you need to surrender to His revelation rather than your reasoning?

The Killed Person Dies by His Appointed Term
Nasafi defines the death of the killed person and refutes al-Ka'bi's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position that a killed person dies by his appointed term, and the distinction between the killer's act and God's creation of death.

Core Idea
The killer's act does not create death; God does, by His custom.

This lesson tackles a key theological question in Islamic debates on predestination: Does the victim of murder die by their decreed lifespan or is their death a violation of that decree? Imam al-Nasafi presents the Ahl al-Sunna view that the killed person indeed dies by his term, carefully distinguishing between the killer's act (qatl) and the death itself (mawt) which is created by God. He also refutes the Mu'tazilite al-Ka'bi's objection. By the end, you will grasp the subtle Maturidi reconciliation of human agency and divine decree.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic text and translation. Note that Nasafi opens with the statement of the Ahl al-Sunna position: the killed person dies by his term.
This is a direct counter to some Mu'tazila views.
2
Distinguish between qatl and mawt
Find where Nasafi defines killing (qatl) as the act of the killer that subsists in him, and death (mawt) as what God creates in the victim as a meaning contrary to life. Understand that these are two separate realities.
3
Understand the role of 'custom' (ijrā' al-ʿāda)
Notice that Nasafi explains how God creates death following the killer's act as a matter of customary operation, not as a necessary effect. This preserves divine sovereignty.
4
Examine al-Ka'bi's objection and note the incomplete response
Al-Ka'bi claimed the killed person does not die because death is from God and killing from the killer. Nasafi's response begins but the passage cuts off. Reflect on why this distinction matters.
The passage ends abruptly; be aware it is incomplete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في الآجال: المقتول عندنا ميت بأجله، وما يخلق الله فيه من المعنى المنافي للحياة قتلاً، كما يسمى مفعول الله تعالى وليس بفعل للقاتل وهو الموت، والقتل فعل القاتل قائم به ليس بحال في المقتول، والفعل الذي يوجد في القاتل ويوجد الله عقيبه انزهاق الروح أو الموت في المحل بطريق إجراء العادة يسمى ما يتفرق عقيبه جسم صلب كسرا، والتفرق مفعول الله تعالى لا صنع للعبد فيه، وزعم الكعبي أن المقتول غير ميت لأن الموت من فعل الله والقتل من فعل القاتل، وقال أجزاء
The discussion on terms (ājāl): The killed person, according to us, dies by his term. And what God creates in him of the meaning contrary to life is killing, just as it is called the act of God Most High and not an act of the killer, and that is death. And killing is an act of the killer, subsisting in him, not inhering in the killed. And the act that exists in the killer and God exists consequent upon it the departure of the soul or death in the locus by way of the running of custom is called that whereby a solid body breaks apart, and the breaking apart is an act of God Most High, no doing of the servant in it. And al-Kaʿbī claimed that the killed person does not die, because death is from God's act and killing is from the killer's act. And he said 'parts' (ajzāʾ).
Source pages p. 950

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of decreed lifespans (ājāl). Nasafi states the Ahl al-Sunna position: the killed person dies by his appointed term (ajal). God creates in the killed person the meaning contrary to life, which is called killing (qatl), but it is an act of God (mafʿūl Allāh) and not an act of the killer. The killer's act is the killing (qatl) itself, which subsists in the killer, not in the victim. The act that occurs in the killer and God creates consequent upon it the departure of the soul (inzihāq al-rūḥ) or death in the locus through the running of custom is called that which causes a solid body to break apart. The breaking apart (tafarruq) is an act of God, with no human doing. Nasafi then mentions al-Kaʿbī's view that the killed person does not die because death is from God's act and killing is from the killer's act. The text ends abruptly with 'and he said parts' (wa qāl ajzāʾ), likely referring to al-Kaʿbī's argument about parts of the body or something similar, but the meaning is incomplete. The passage establishes the fundamental distinction between the act of God (creating death) and the act of the killer (the killing act), and affirms that the killed person's death is by his decreed term, contrary to some Muʿtazila views.

Key Terms
أجل
موت
قتل
إجراء العادة
انزهاق الروح
تفرق
كعبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The killed person's death is solely caused by the killer, not by divine decree.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that while the killer performs the act of killing, death itself is created by God by His custom, so the killed person dies by his appointed term.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the relationship between the killer's act and the death of the killed person?
A) The killer creates death directly in the victim.
B) The killer's act is the cause of death, but death itself is created by God following the custom.
C) The killer's act is irrelevant; only God causes death directly.
D) The killed person does not actually die; death is an illusion.

Key Takeaway
The killed person dies by his appointed term; God creates death as a consequence of the killer's act through His custom, preserving both divine decree and human responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a sudden death or a tragedy caused by human actions, does your heart truly rest in the belief that it was by God's term, or do you inwardly accuse or question the decree? This lesson demands you examine whether your trust in qadr is real when tested. What concrete step will you take this week to strengthen that trust?
?
Al-Ka'bi's error was to separate God's creation of death from the killer's act so radically that the killer had no role. In your own life, are there situations where you deny your own accountability by blaming everything on 'God's will'? How does Nasafi's model of custom-based causality help you hold both divine sovereignty and personal responsibility without excuses?
?
The killer's act is only the occasion; God creates the death. Does this make you reconsider your anger towards those who have harmed you? If death is from God alone, can you release your bitterness and see the decree as from Him? Commit to forgive one person this week in light of this truth.

The Maturidi View on the Term of the Killed Person
Nasafi defines death and killing in the Maturidi framework.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position that the killed person dies by his appointed term, and how death is an act of God while killing is an act of the killer.

Core Idea
Death is God's creation; killing is the killer's act, not the cause of death.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi lays out the Maturidi doctrine on the appointed term (ajal) of the killed person. He distinguishes between death as God's direct creation and killing as the human act that accompanies it. This distinction is central to refuting the Mu'tazila theory of generated acts. Read the passage to grasp the precise definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the definitions of death (al-mawt) and killing (al-qatl).
2
Identify the two acts
Notice the distinction: God creates death, while the killer performs the act of killing. The killer's act does not cause death directly.
The phrase 'التفرق مفعول الله تعالى لا صنع للعبد فيه' emphasizes God's exclusive role in creating effects.
3
Reflect on the term
The killed person dies by his own term (ajal). Reflect on how this upholds divine predestination while preserving human responsibility for the killing act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في الآجال: المقتول عندنا ميت بأجله، وما يخلق الله فيه من المعنى المنافي للحياة قتلاً، كما يسمى مفعول الله تعالى وليس بفعل للقاتل وهو الموت، والقتل فعل القاتل قائم به ليس بحال في المقتول، والفعل الذي يوجد في القاتل ويوجد الله عقيبه انزهاق الروح أو الموت في المحل بطريق إجراء العادة يسمى ما يتفرق عقيبه جسم صلب كسرا، والتفرق مفعول الله تعالى لا صنع للعبد فيه، وزعم الكعبي أن المقتول غير ميت لأن الموت من فعل الله والقتل من فعل القاتل، وقال غيره من المعتزلة في المقتول معنيان: أحدهما من الله وهو الموت، والآخر من العبد وهو القتل، وما ذكرنا من دلائل إبطال القول بالتولد يوجب بطلان هذا كله، ثم يتصل بهذا أن المقتول ميت بأجله وهذا هو أجله لا أجل له سواه، وكذا قال أبو الهذيل من جملة المعتزلة حتى قال: لو لم يقتل لمات بأجله في وقت قتله قال : والمدة التي لم يعش إليها لم تكن أجلاً ولا من عمره، وعندنا ليس الأمر كذلك بل يقتل لا محالة، وكذا قال الجبائي أن لا أجل له إلا هذا وقال الباقون المقتول مقطوع عليه أجله كذا ذكروا
Discussion on the terms of life (al-ājāl): The killed person (al-maqtūl) according to us dies by his term (ajal). What God creates in him of the meaning contrary to life is killing, just as what is called the effect of God the Exalted, and it is not an act of the killer. That is death. Killing is the act of the killer, subsisting in him, not occurring in the killed person. The act that exists in the killer, and God creates after it the departure of the soul or death in the locus by way of the running of the habit, is called what disintegrates after it a solid body in pieces. The disintegration is an effect of God the Exalted, no act of the servant in it. al-Ka‘bī claimed that the killed person does not die, because death is from God’s act and killing is from the killer’s act. Others of the Mu‘tazila said concerning the killed person two meanings: one from God, which is death, and the other from the servant, which is killing. What we mentioned of the proofs of invalidating the statement of generated acts (tawallud) necessitates the invalidity of all this. Then connected to this is that the killed person dies by his term, and this is his term, no other term for him. Likewise, Abu al-Hudhayl from among the Mu‘tazila said, and even said: If he were not killed, he would die by his term at the time of his killing. He said: The period which he did not live to was not a term nor from his lifespan. According to us, it is not like that; rather he is killed inevitably. Likewise, al-Jubbā’ī said that there is no term for him except this. And the rest said: The killed person has his term cut off. Thus they mentioned.
Source pages p. 950

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argumentation about the appointed term (ajal) of the killed person. The Maturidi position is first stated: the killed person dies by his term. What God creates in the killed person that is contrary to life is called killing, but the actual death is an act of God, not an act of the killer. The killer’s act is the killing, which subsists in the killer and does not occur in the killed person. The act of the killer (like striking) is followed by God creating the departure of the soul or death in the locus by the continuous habit of God. The disintegration of the body is an effect of God. The passage then critiques the Mu‘tazila, first al-Ka‘bī who claimed that the killed person does not die because death is from God and killing from the killer. Others said the killed person has two meanings: death from God and killing from the servant. The author asserts that the earlier proofs against tawallud invalidate these views. Then the Maturidi point is reiterated: the killed person dies by his term, and this is his only term. The views of Abu al-Hudhayl (who agreed but with a nuance that if not killed he would die at that time anyway) and al-Jubbā’ī (who said no other term) are noted, and the rest held that the term is cut off. The passage contrasts their own view that the killed person is killed inevitably (yughtalu la mahāla).

Key Terms
الأجل
المقتول
الموت
القتل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the killer directly causes death by generating an effect.
Correction: The Maturidi view holds that killing is the human act, but God creates death after it. The killer does not produce death directly.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi view in this passage, who creates death?
A) The killer
B) God
C) The killed person
D) Both God and the killer

Key Takeaway
Death is God's creation; the killer's act is merely the accompanying human action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you or a loved one were killed, would you truly accept that it was your appointed term from God, or would you feel resentment toward the killer? How would you reconcile the killer's blameworthiness with God's decree in your heart?
?
When you witness the death of someone through violence or accident, do you instinctively blame the circumstances, or do you submit that God alone gives and takes life? What does this reveal about the depth of your tawhid?
?
Do you live each day fully aware that your term is fixed by God, not by your health choices or risks? How would your actions change if you truly internalized that no one can hasten or delay your death?

Critique of Mu'tazila Views on the Term of the Killed Person
Nasafi critiques Mu'tazila views and reaffirms the Maturidi stance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various Mu'tazila opinions on the term of the killed person and how the Maturidi view refutes them.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila views on the term are inconsistent with divine predestination.

After establishing the Maturidi position, Imam al-Nasafi turns to critique the Mu'tazila. He presents the views of al-Ka‘bī, Abu al-Hudhayl, al-Jubbā’ī, and others, showing how their attempts to separate death and killing lead to contradictions. The passage reaffirms that the killed person inevitably dies by his one and only term.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila opinions
Read the passage and identify the different Mu'tazila views: al-Ka‘bī, the two-meaning view, Abu al-Hudhayl, al-Jubbā’ī, and the rest.
2
Note the refutation
The author invokes earlier proofs against tawallud (generated acts) to invalidate these views. Understand why tawallud is rejected.
3
Compare Abu al-Hudhayl's statement
Abu al-Hudhayl says if not killed, the person would die at the same time. The Maturidi view contradicts this: 'rather he is killed inevitably'.
Contrast the hypothetical with the Maturidi affirmation of predestination.
4
Synthesize the Maturidi position
The passage ends by reiterating that the killed person has only one term. Summarize how this differs from all Mu'tazila opinions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في الآجال: المقتول عندنا ميت بأجله، وما يخلق الله فيه من المعنى المنافي للحياة قتلاً، كما يسمى مفعول الله تعالى وليس بفعل للقاتل وهو الموت، والقتل فعل القاتل قائم به ليس بحال في المقتول، والفعل الذي يوجد في القاتل ويوجد الله عقيبه انزهاق الروح أو الموت في المحل بطريق إجراء العادة يسمى ما يتفرق عقيبه جسم صلب كسرا، والتفرق مفعول الله تعالى لا صنع للعبد فيه، وزعم الكعبي أن المقتول غير ميت لأن الموت من فعل الله والقتل من فعل القاتل، وقال غيره من المعتزلة في المقتول معنيان: أحدهما من الله وهو الموت، والآخر من العبد وهو القتل، وما ذكرنا من دلائل إبطال القول بالتولد يوجب بطلان هذا كله، ثم يتصل بهذا أن المقتول ميت بأجله وهذا هو أجله لا أجل له سواه، وكذا قال أبو الهذيل من جملة المعتزلة حتى قال: لو لم يقتل لمات بأجله في وقت قتله قال : والمدة التي لم يعش إليها لم تكن أجلاً ولا من عمره، وعندنا ليس الأمر كذلك بل يقتل لا محالة، وكذا قال الجبائي أن لا أجل له إلا هذا وقال الباقون المقتول مقطوع عليه أجله كذا ذكروا
Discussion on the terms of life (al-ājāl): The killed person (al-maqtūl) according to us dies by his term (ajal). What God creates in him of the meaning contrary to life is killing, just as what is called the effect of God the Exalted, and it is not an act of the killer. That is death. Killing is the act of the killer, subsisting in him, not occurring in the killed person. The act that exists in the killer, and God creates after it the departure of the soul or death in the locus by way of the running of the habit, is called what disintegrates after it a solid body in pieces. The disintegration is an effect of God the Exalted, no act of the servant in it. al-Ka‘bī claimed that the killed person does not die, because death is from God’s act and killing is from the killer’s act. Others of the Mu‘tazila said concerning the killed person two meanings: one from God, which is death, and the other from the servant, which is killing. What we mentioned of the proofs of invalidating the statement of generated acts (tawallud) necessitates the invalidity of all this. Then connected to this is that the killed person dies by his term, and this is his term, no other term for him. Likewise, Abu al-Hudhayl from among the Mu‘tazila said, and even said: If he were not killed, he would die by his term at the time of his killing. He said: The period which he did not live to was not a term nor from his lifespan. According to us, it is not like that; rather he is killed inevitably. Likewise, al-Jubbā’ī said that there is no term for him except this. And the rest said: The killed person has his term cut off. Thus they mentioned.
Source pages p. 950

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argumentation about the appointed term (ajal) of the killed person. The Maturidi position is first stated: the killed person dies by his term. What God creates in the killed person that is contrary to life is called killing, but the actual death is an act of God, not an act of the killer. The killer’s act is the killing, which subsists in the killer and does not occur in the killed person. The act of the killer (like striking) is followed by God creating the departure of the soul or death in the locus by the continuous habit of God. The disintegration of the body is an effect of God. The passage then critiques the Mu‘tazila, first al-Ka‘bī who claimed that the killed person does not die because death is from God and killing from the killer. Others said the killed person has two meanings: death from God and killing from the servant. The author asserts that the earlier proofs against tawallud invalidate these views. Then the Maturidi point is reiterated: the killed person dies by his term, and this is his only term. The views of Abu al-Hudhayl (who agreed but with a nuance that if not killed he would die at that time anyway) and al-Jubbā’ī (who said no other term) are noted, and the rest held that the term is cut off. The passage contrasts their own view that the killed person is killed inevitably (yughtalu la mahāla).

Key Terms
الكعبي
أبو الهذيل
الجبائي
التولد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the killed person has two different terms or that killing can alter the term.
Correction: The Maturidi view asserts that the killed person has only one term, and he dies by it inevitably; killing does not change it.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi view in the passage, is the killed person's term affected by the killing?
A) Yes, it is cut off.
B) No, it is his only term.
C) It depends on the killer's intention.
D) There is no term after killing.

Key Takeaway
The killed person's term is fixed by God and not altered by the act of killing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every moment of your life—including the manner of your death—is fixed by God, even if someone were to harm you? How does that belief change the way you fear or trust in Allah's decree?
?
When you take precautions to protect your life, do you act as if these measures can override God's decree, or do you acknowledge that nothing can change what has been written? Examine your heart: where does your real reliance lie?
?
This lesson teaches that the killed person's term is unchanged by the act of killing. If that is true, how does it affect your view of injustice and suffering in the world? Can you accept that even the most tragic death is within God's perfect plan, or does that unsettle you? What would it take for you to surrender to that truth?

intermediate
8 min
The Maturidi position on the term of the murdered
Al-Nasafi presents Mu'tazila positions and the Maturidi counter-position with proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi stance that the murdered person dies by his own fixed term and the evidence for it from Quran and hadith, contrasted with Mu'tazila views.

Core Idea
The murdered dies by his own term; no other term exists.

Imam al-Nasafi opens this discussion by presenting the Mu'tazila view that distinguishes between death from God and killing from the servant. He then asserts the Maturidi position: the murdered person dies by his own term, and this is his only term. He supports this with a Quranic verse and a well-known hadith. This lesson introduces the core doctrinal difference.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila positions
Identify the two meanings the Mu'tazila assign to the murdered person: death from God and killing from the servant. Note the views of Abu al-Hudhayl, al-Jubbai, and other Mu'tazila.
Abu al-Hudhayl argues that if not killed, the person would die at the same time.
2
Find the Maturidi stance
Al-Nasafi states: 'the murdered dies by his term, and this is his term; there is no term for him other than it.' Notice the emphasis on inevitability: 'he is killed inevitably'.
This directly opposes the Mu'tazila duality.
3
Examine the proofs
Read the Quranic verse (7:34) and the hadith about the angel writing the term. How do these support a single, fixed term?
The verse states that when the term comes, no delay or advance is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غيره من المعتزلة في المقتول معنيان: أحدهما وهو الموت، والآخر من العبد وهو القتل، وما ذكرنا من الله دلائل إبطال القول بالتولد يوجب بطلان هذا كله، ثم يتصل بهذا أن المقتول ميت بأجله وهذا هو أجله لا أجل له سواه، وكذا قال أبو الهذيل من جملة المعتزلة حتى قال: لو لم يقتل لمات بأجله في وقت قتله قال: والمدة التي لم يعش إليها لم تكن أجلاً ولا من عمره، وعندنا ليس الأمر كذلك بل يقتل لا محالة، وكذا قال الجبائي أن لا أجل له إلا هذا وقال الباقون المقتول مقطوع عليه أجله كذا ذكروا، ولنا قوله: (فَإِذَا جَاءَ أَجَلُهُمْ لَا يَسْتَأْخِرُونَ سَاعَةً وَلَا يَسْتَقْدِمُونَ) والحديث المعروف أن عند تصوير العبد في بطن أمه يأمر الله ملكا فيكتب على جبهته رزقه وأجله وسعادته وشقاوته، وتعلقهم بقوله تعالى: (وَمَا يُعَمَّرُ مِنْ مُعَمَّرٍ وَلَا يُنْقَصُ مِنْ عُمُرِهِ إِلَّا فِي كِتَابٍ) وتقدير الآية عندنا والله أعلم - وما يعمر من معمر، ولا ينقص من عمر معمر آخر سواه أي يعطي لهذا الثاني من العمر ما يكون ناقصا بمقابلة عمر الأول والهاء راجع إلى من يماثله في الاسم لا إلى عين المذكور كما يقال: هذا درهم ونصفه أي نصف درهم آخر مماثل للأول في الاسم كذا ذكر كبار أهل اللغة منهم الفراء، ويقولون: لو كان مات بأجله لم يجب القصاص ولا وجب الضمان إذا كان شاه فذبحها لأنه أحسن إليه، والجواب: أن وجوب القصاص والضمان لارتكابه النهي باكتسابه الفعل الذي أجرى الله تعالى العادة بتخليقه الموت عقبه لا على ما وجد في المحل من المعنى المنافي للحياة على ما مر في مسألة التولد. يحققه أن عندهم كان من الجائز أن يكون هذا أجله إن لم يكن واجبًا، والضمان لا يجب مع الشك ولا القصاص، ومع وجب هاهنا دل أنه إنما وجب لما ذكرنا ثم الحقيقة أنه لا خلاف في هذه المسألة لأنهم يقولون: إن معلوم الله تعالى في انتهاء عمره غير هذا إذ لا يظن هذا بمن خالف هشام بن عمرو من تجويز كون المعدوم معلومًا، وإنما يقولون: إن الله تعالى يعلم أنه يقتل لا محالة ويعلم أنه لو لم يقتل لعاش إلى وقت كذا على الأصل أنه تعالى يعلم ما يكون، ويعلم أن ما لا يكون لو كان كيف كان يكون على ما قال في حق الكفار: (وَلَوْ رُدُّوا لَعَادُوا لِمَا نُهُوا عَنْهُ) وإن كان يعلم أنهم لا يردون ونحن ننكر هذا وإنما ننكر كون ذلك مشكوكاً مجهولاً عنده كما يكون ممن يجهل العواقب وهم أيضا يأبون هذا والله الموفق
Others among the Mu'tazila regarding the murdered [person] have two meanings: one of them is death, and the other from the servant is killing. And what we have mentioned of proofs from God for invalidating the statement of generation (tawallud) necessitates the invalidation of all this. Then connected to this is that the murdered [person] dies by his term (ajal), and this is his term; there is no term for him other than it. Likewise said Abu al-Hudhayl among the Mu'tazila, until he said: If he were not killed, he would die by his term at the time of his killing. He said: And the period which he did not live to was not a term nor from his life. And with us it is not like that; rather he is killed inevitably. Likewise said al-Jubbai that there is no term for him except this. And the rest said: The murdered [person] is cut off from his term, as they mentioned. And for us is His statement: 'Then when their term comes, they cannot delay an hour nor advance [it]' (7:34), and the well-known hadith that when the servant is formed in his mother's womb, God commands an angel who writes on his forehead his provision (rizq), his term (ajal), his happiness and his misery. And their [the Mu'tazila's] clinging to His statement, the Exalted: 'And no one is given a long life nor is anything decreased from his life except in a Book' (35:11), and the interpretation of the verse with us – and God knows best – is 'And no one is given a long life nor is the life of another similar to him decreased' i.e., it gives to this second one from the life what is deficient corresponding to the life of the first. And the pronoun refers to one similar to him in name, not to the very one mentioned, as is said: 'This is a dirham and half of it', i.e., another half dirham similar to the first in name. Thus mentioned the senior scholars of language among them al-Farra. And they [the Mu'tazila] say: If he had died by his term, retaliation (qisas) would not be obligatory nor would compensation (daman) be obligatory if he slaughtered a sheep and killed it because he would have done good to it. And the answer: That the obligation of retaliation and compensation is because of his committing the prohibition by his acquisition (iktisab) of the act which God the Exalted has made customary (jara al-'adah) so that He creates death consequent to it, not because of what existed in the locus of the meaning contrary to life, as passed in the question of generation (tawallud). This is verified by the fact that with them it was possible that this could be his term if it were not obligatory. And compensation is not obligatory with doubt, nor retaliation. And when it is obligatory here, it indicates that it is obligatory only for what we mentioned. Then the reality is that there is no disagreement in this matter because they say: Indeed, what is known by God the Exalted regarding the end of his life is other than this, since this is not thought of one who opposed Hisham ibn 'Amr concerning the permissibility of the non-existent being known. Rather they say: God the Exalted knows that he will be killed inevitably, and He knows that if he had not been killed he would have lived until such a time, based on the principle that He the Exalted knows what will be, and He knows that if what is not were to be, how it would be, as He said regarding the disbelievers: 'And if they were returned, they would return to what they were forbidden' (6:28), although He knows that they will not be returned. And we deny this; we only deny that it is doubtful and unknown to Him as is the case with one who is ignorant of consequences. And they also reject this. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 951

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the term (ajal) of a murdered person, typical of kalam discussions on divine decree and human action. The author, Imam al-Nasafi, is refuting Mu'tazila views that distinguish between death from God and killing from the servant, a position linked to their doctrine of generation (tawallud). He begins by noting that the Mu'tazila conceive the murdered person as having two aspects: death (from God) and killing (from the servant). Al-Nasafi states that his earlier proof against tawallud invalidates this duality. He then presents the core Maturidi position: the murdered person dies by his own term, and this is his only term. He cites Abu al-Hudhayl who similarly argued that if not killed, he would die at the same time, and that the un-lived period was not part of his term. Al-Nasafi disagrees, insisting the person is killed inevitably. He also mentions al-Jubbai's agreement and notes that other Mu'tazila hold that the murdered is cut off from his term. Al-Nasafi then provides his own proof: the Quranic verse (7:34) that when a term comes, no one can delay or advance it, and the well-known hadith about the angel writing the term on the fetus's forehead. This supports the notion that the term is fixed and singular. Next, the author addresses the Mu'tazila's proof from the verse (35:11) about not decreasing a life. He interprets it as referring to two different persons with similar names, not a single individual: the pronoun 'his' in the verse refers to another similar person, not the same one. He supports this with a linguistic analogy from al-Farra. Then the Mu'tazila raise an objection: if the murdered dies by his term, then retaliation (qisas) and compensation (daman) would not be obligatory because the murderer would not have caused death. Al-Nasafi answers that obligation of qisas and daman arises because the murderer committed a prohibited act that God has made customary to cause death, not because he actually caused death. He explains that if the term were uncertain, qisas would not be obligatory, but since it is obligatory, it is due to the act's prohibited nature. Finally, al-Nasafi clarifies that the real dispute is not about God's knowledge: both sides agree God knows what will happen. The Mu'tazila believe God knows the actual outcome and also knows what would have happened if the murderer had not acted (counterfactuals). Al-Nasafi states that Maturidis deny that God knows such counterfactual possibilities as 'if he had not been killed, he would have lived longer', not because they are unknown to God, but because they consider such knowledge impossible since the non-existent is not an object of knowledge. He references the Quranic verse (6:28) where God says the disbelievers would return if returned, though He knows they will not be returned. Al-Nasafi concludes that Maturidis reject that future possibilities are doubtful to God; rather they affirm God's complete knowledge of all things.

Key Terms
أجل
مقتول
تولد
قصاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that the murdered person has two aspects (death from God and killing from the servant) and that the term can be different.
Correction: The Maturidi position is that the murdered person dies by his own single, fixed term, as supported by Quran and hadith.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi position in this passage, what happens if a person is killed?
A) He dies by his own term, which is the same as if he had not been killed.
B) He dies by a term that is different from his original term.
C) He dies partly by God's death and partly by the servant's killing.
D) His term is cut short by the killing.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi view is that every person, including the murdered, has a single fixed term that cannot be changed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that your own death is fixed at a single, unchangeable moment, why do you still fear situations that could harm you? Does your daily anxiety reflect trust in God's decree or a hidden disbelief in this teaching?
?
The angel writes your term, provision, happiness, and misery before you are born. Which of these four do you worry about most, and how does that worry expose a lack of reliance on Allah's writing?
?
When you hear of a murder, do you feel a sense of injustice against the victim, or do you see it as the unfolding of their fixed term? How can you reconcile holding the murderer accountable while submitting to Allah's decree?

Refuting the Mu'tazila interpretation of verse 35:11
Al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila interpretation of verse 35:11.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi scholars interpret the Quranic verse about decreasing life, and how they refute the Mu'tazila claim that the term can be shortened.

Core Idea
The pronoun in the verse refers to another similar person, not the same one.

The Mu'tazila cite verse 35:11 to argue that a person's term can be decreased. Al-Nasafi refutes this by providing an alternative interpretation: the verse speaks of two different persons, not a single individual. He supports this with linguistic evidence from al-Farra. This lesson clarifies this key exegetical difference.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila proof
The Mu'tazila cling to the verse 'And no one is given a long life nor is anything decreased from his life except in a Book'. They claim this shows that a person's life can be shortened.
They interpret 'decreased from his life' as referring to the same person.
2
Understand the Maturidi interpretation
Al-Nasafi explains that the pronoun 'his' refers to another person similar to the first, not the same person. So the verse means: the life of one is decreased to give to another.
The linguistic analogy: 'this is a dirham and half of it' means another half dirham.
3
Learn the scholarly support
Al-Nasafi cites al-Farra and other senior linguists to support this interpretation.
This exegesis removes the proof for term reduction.

Source Text

He interprets it as referring to two different persons with similar names, not a single individual: the pronoun 'his' in the verse refers to another similar person, not the same one. He supports this with a linguistic analogy from al-Farra. Then the Mu'tazila raise an objection: if the murdered dies by his term, then retaliation (qisas) and compensation (daman) would not be obligatory because the murderer would not have caused death. Al-Nasafi answers that obligation of qisas and daman arises because the murderer committed a prohibited act that God has made customary to cause death, not because he actually caused death. He explains that if the term were uncertain, qisas would not be obligatory, but since it is obligatory, it is due to the act's prohibited nature. Finally, al-Nasafi clarifies that the real dispute is not about God's knowledge: both sides agree God knows what will happen. The Mu'tazila believe God knows the actual outcome and also knows what would have happened if the murderer had not acted (counterfactuals). Al-Nasafi states that Maturidis deny that God knows such counterfactual possibilities as 'if he had not been killed, he would have lived longer', not because they are unknown to God, but because they consider such knowledge impossible since the non-existent is not an object of knowledge. He references the Quranic verse (6:28) where God says the disbelievers would return if returned, though He knows they will not be returned. Al-Nasafi concludes that Maturidis reject that future possibilities are doubtful to God; rather they affirm God's complete knowledge of all things.

Key Terms
عمر
كتاب
تأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe the verse (35:11) proves that a person's term can be shortened.
Correction: The Maturidi interpretation is that the verse speaks of two different persons; one receives a long life, another a shorter life, not a reduction of the same life.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi's interpretation, what does the pronoun 'his' in 'decreased from his life' refer to?
A) The same person who was given a long life.
B) A different person similar in name to the first.
C) The murderer.
D) The angel who writes the term.

Key Takeaway
The verse does not support term reduction; it refers to different people having different lifespans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself thinking that your circumstances—like your lifespan or health—are within your control, and then using that to excuse inaction in obedience? How does al-Nasafi's explanation that even the length of life is decreed challenge that comfort zone? What will you change today to rely more on Allah's decree than on your own planning?
?
When you read a verse that seems to contradict your understanding of qadar, do you quickly accept a reinterpretation that fits your views, or do you submit to the apparent meaning? This lesson shows that careful linguistic analysis can clarify the intended meaning. Are you willing to learn from scholars like al-Nasafi even when it challenges your initial reading? What step can you take this week to deepen your trust in the scholars of Ahl al-Sunna?
?
The Mu'tazila used this verse to argue that a murderer shortens the victim's life. Do you ever subtly blame others for your misfortunes, as if they could change what Allah has decreed for you? How does al-Nasafi's refutation help you trust in Allah's decree and let go of resentment? Write one specific situation where you can apply this today.

The obligation of retaliation despite a fixed term
Al-Nasafi answers the Mu'tazila objection that qisas would not be required if the term is fixed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why retaliation (qisas) and compensation (daman) are obligatory even though the victim dies by his own fixed term, and the Maturidi answer to the Mu'tazila objection.

Core Idea
Qisas is for committing the prohibited act, not for causing death.

The Mu'tazila object that if the murdered dies by his term, then the murderer did not cause death, so why is retaliation obligatory? Al-Nasafi answers that the obligation arises from the act itself being prohibited and from God's custom of creating death after such acts. This lesson explains this key theological distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila objection
They argue that if the victim dies by his term, the murderer did not cause death, so qisas and daman should not be obligatory. They also extend this to slaughtering a sheep.
This objection is based on their understanding of causality.
2
Understand the Maturidi answer
Al-Nasafi says the obligation is because the murderer committed a prohibited act (the killing) that God customarily follows with death. It is not because the act itself produced death.
This is consistent with the Maturidi rejection of secondary causality.
3
Analyze the logic
If the term were uncertain, qisas would not be obligatory. Since it is obligatory, it must be due to the act's prohibition, not its effect.
The certainty of the term does not negate the prohibition.
