غيره من المعتزلة في المقتول معنيان: أحدهما وهو الموت، والآخر من العبد وهو القتل، وما ذكرنا من الله دلائل إبطال القول بالتولد يوجب بطلان هذا كله، ثم يتصل بهذا أن المقتول ميت بأجله وهذا هو أجله لا أجل له سواه، وكذا قال أبو الهذيل من جملة المعتزلة حتى قال: لو لم يقتل لمات بأجله في وقت قتله قال: والمدة التي لم يعش إليها لم تكن أجلاً ولا من عمره، وعندنا ليس الأمر كذلك بل يقتل لا محالة، وكذا قال الجبائي أن لا أجل له إلا هذا وقال الباقون المقتول مقطوع عليه أجله كذا ذكروا، ولنا قوله: (فَإِذَا جَاءَ أَجَلُهُمْ لَا يَسْتَأْخِرُونَ سَاعَةً وَلَا يَسْتَقْدِمُونَ) والحديث المعروف أن عند تصوير العبد في بطن أمه يأمر الله ملكا فيكتب على جبهته رزقه وأجله وسعادته وشقاوته، وتعلقهم بقوله تعالى: (وَمَا يُعَمَّرُ مِنْ مُعَمَّرٍ وَلَا يُنْقَصُ مِنْ عُمُرِهِ إِلَّا فِي كِتَابٍ) وتقدير الآية عندنا والله أعلم - وما يعمر من معمر، ولا ينقص من عمر معمر آخر سواه أي يعطي لهذا الثاني من العمر ما يكون ناقصا بمقابلة عمر الأول والهاء راجع إلى من يماثله في الاسم لا إلى عين المذكور كما يقال: هذا درهم ونصفه أي نصف درهم آخر مماثل للأول في الاسم كذا ذكر كبار أهل اللغة منهم الفراء، ويقولون: لو كان مات بأجله لم يجب القصاص ولا وجب الضمان إذا كان شاه فذبحها لأنه أحسن إليه، والجواب: أن وجوب القصاص والضمان لارتكابه النهي باكتسابه الفعل الذي أجرى الله تعالى العادة بتخليقه الموت عقبه لا على ما وجد في المحل من المعنى المنافي للحياة على ما مر في مسألة التولد. يحققه أن عندهم كان من الجائز أن يكون هذا أجله إن لم يكن واجبًا، والضمان لا يجب مع الشك ولا القصاص، ومع وجب هاهنا دل أنه إنما وجب لما ذكرنا ثم الحقيقة أنه لا خلاف في هذه المسألة لأنهم يقولون: إن معلوم الله تعالى في انتهاء عمره غير هذا إذ لا يظن هذا بمن خالف هشام بن عمرو من تجويز كون المعدوم معلومًا، وإنما يقولون: إن الله تعالى يعلم أنه يقتل لا محالة ويعلم أنه لو لم يقتل لعاش إلى وقت كذا على الأصل أنه تعالى يعلم ما يكون، ويعلم أن ما لا يكون لو كان كيف كان يكون على ما قال في حق الكفار: (وَلَوْ رُدُّوا لَعَادُوا لِمَا نُهُوا عَنْهُ) وإن كان يعلم أنهم لا يردون ونحن ننكر هذا وإنما ننكر كون ذلك مشكوكاً مجهولاً عنده كما يكون ممن يجهل العواقب وهم أيضا يأبون هذا والله الموفق
Others among the Mu'tazila regarding the murdered [person] have two meanings: one of them is death, and the other from the servant is killing. And what we have mentioned of proofs from God for invalidating the statement of generation (tawallud) necessitates the invalidation of all this. Then connected to this is that the murdered [person] dies by his term (ajal), and this is his term; there is no term for him other than it. Likewise said Abu al-Hudhayl among the Mu'tazila, until he said: If he were not killed, he would die by his term at the time of his killing. He said: And the period which he did not live to was not a term nor from his life. And with us it is not like that; rather he is killed inevitably. Likewise said al-Jubbai that there is no term for him except this. And the rest said: The murdered [person] is cut off from his term, as they mentioned. And for us is His statement: 'Then when their term comes, they cannot delay an hour nor advance [it]' (7:34), and the well-known hadith that when the servant is formed in his mother's womb, God commands an angel who writes on his forehead his provision (rizq), his term (ajal), his happiness and his misery. And their [the Mu'tazila's] clinging to His statement, the Exalted: 'And no one is given a long life nor is anything decreased from his life except in a Book' (35:11), and the interpretation of the verse with us – and God knows best – is 'And no one is given a long life nor is the life of another similar to him decreased' i.e., it gives to this second one from the life what is deficient corresponding to the life of the first. And the pronoun refers to one similar to him in name, not to the very one mentioned, as is said: 'This is a dirham and half of it', i.e., another half dirham similar to the first in name. Thus mentioned the senior scholars of language among them al-Farra. And they [the Mu'tazila] say: If he had died by his term, retaliation (qisas) would not be obligatory nor would compensation (daman) be obligatory if he slaughtered a sheep and killed it because he would have done good to it. And the answer: That the obligation of retaliation and compensation is because of his committing the prohibition by his acquisition (iktisab) of the act which God the Exalted has made customary (jara al-'adah) so that He creates death consequent to it, not because of what existed in the locus of the meaning contrary to life, as passed in the question of generation (tawallud). This is verified by the fact that with them it was possible that this could be his term if it were not obligatory. And compensation is not obligatory with doubt, nor retaliation. And when it is obligatory here, it indicates that it is obligatory only for what we mentioned. Then the reality is that there is no disagreement in this matter because they say: Indeed, what is known by God the Exalted regarding the end of his life is other than this, since this is not thought of one who opposed Hisham ibn 'Amr concerning the permissibility of the non-existent being known. Rather they say: God the Exalted knows that he will be killed inevitably, and He knows that if he had not been killed he would have lived until such a time, based on the principle that He the Exalted knows what will be, and He knows that if what is not were to be, how it would be, as He said regarding the disbelievers: 'And if they were returned, they would return to what they were forbidden' (6:28), although He knows that they will not be returned. And we deny this; we only deny that it is doubtful and unknown to Him as is the case with one who is ignorant of consequences. And they also reject this. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 951

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the term (ajal) of a murdered person, typical of kalam discussions on divine decree and human action. The author, Imam al-Nasafi, is refuting Mu'tazila views that distinguish between death from God and killing from the servant, a position linked to their doctrine of generation (tawallud). He begins by noting that the Mu'tazila conceive the murdered person as having two aspects: death (from God) and killing (from the servant). Al-Nasafi states that his earlier proof against tawallud invalidates this duality. He then presents the core Maturidi position: the murdered person dies by his own term, and this is his only term. He cites Abu al-Hudhayl who similarly argued that if not killed, he would die at the same time, and that the un-lived period was not part of his term. Al-Nasafi disagrees, insisting the person is killed inevitably. He also mentions al-Jubbai's agreement and notes that other Mu'tazila hold that the murdered is cut off from his term. Al-Nasafi then provides his own proof: the Quranic verse (7:34) that when a term comes, no one can delay or advance it, and the well-known hadith about the angel writing the term on the fetus's forehead. This supports the notion that the term is fixed and singular. Next, the author addresses the Mu'tazila's proof from the verse (35:11) about not decreasing a life. He interprets it as referring to two different persons with similar names, not a single individual: the pronoun 'his' in the verse refers to another similar person, not the same one. He supports this with a linguistic analogy from al-Farra. Then the Mu'tazila raise an objection: if the murdered dies by his term, then retaliation (qisas) and compensation (daman) would not be obligatory because the murderer would not have caused death. Al-Nasafi answers that obligation of qisas and daman arises because the murderer committed a prohibited act that God has made customary to cause death, not because he actually caused death. He explains that if the term were uncertain, qisas would not be obligatory, but since it is obligatory, it is due to the act's prohibited nature. Finally, al-Nasafi clarifies that the real dispute is not about God's knowledge: both sides agree God knows what will happen. The Mu'tazila believe God knows the actual outcome and also knows what would have happened if the murderer had not acted (counterfactuals). Al-Nasafi states that Maturidis deny that God knows such counterfactual possibilities as 'if he had not been killed, he would have lived longer', not because they are unknown to God, but because they consider such knowledge impossible since the non-existent is not an object of knowledge. He references the Quranic verse (6:28) where God says the disbelievers would return if returned, though He knows they will not be returned. Al-Nasafi concludes that Maturidis reject that future possibilities are doubtful to God; rather they affirm God's complete knowledge of all things.

نهي
عادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila think that if the term is fixed, the murderer is not liable because he did not cause death.
Correction: The Maturidi view is that the murderer is liable because he committed a prohibited act that God customarily follows with death. The act itself is the cause of punishment.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, why is retaliation (qisas) obligatory for the murderer even though the victim dies by his term?
A) Because the murderer shortened the victim's term.
B) Because the murderer committed a prohibited act that God customarily follows with death.
C) Because the victim's term was not fixed until the murder occurred.
D) Because the murderer has independent power to cause death.

Key Takeaway
Punishment for murder is based on committing the forbidden act, not on causing the victim's death.

?
When you commit a sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking 'it was meant to happen' or 'God wrote it'? How does al-Nasafi's insistence that punishment is for the forbidden act, not the outcome, challenge that excuse?

The Objection and Answer Regarding the Appointed Term
Nasafi answers a Mu'tazili objection about the appointed term and states the real agreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's answer to the Mu'tazili objection about the appointed term and the underlying agreement between both schools.

Core Idea
Liability is for violating God's command, not for changing the appointed term.

This passage addresses a crucial objection from the Mu'tazila: if death occurs at its appointed term, why should a killer be liable? Nasafi's answer reveals the deeper theological commitments about God's knowledge of possibilities and the causality of human acts. By examining his response, we see how Maturidi thought navigates the relationship between God's decree and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the Mu'tazili argument that if the appointed term is fixed, then killing cannot change it, so no liability.
Note the example of slaughtering a sheep as a favor.
2
Identify Nasafi's answer
He says liability is for the prohibited act, not for changing the term.
The answer invokes the concept of acquisition (iktisab).
3
Analyze the verification with generated acts
He references the theory of generated acts (tawallud) to explain that God creates death after the act, not because of a change in term.
This shows that the act is a cause in the customary sense.
4
Understand the agreement about knowledge
Both sides agree God knows what would have happened if the killer had not acted.
The Mu'tazila use the principle of 'what if' knowledge.
5
Note the denial of ignorance
Nasafi emphasizes that God's knowledge is not doubtful, unlike human knowledge.
This resolves the apparent disagreement.

منهم الفراء ويقولون لو كان مات بأجله لم يجب القصاص ولا وجب الضمان إذا كان شاه فذبحها لأنه أحسن إليه والجواب أن وجوب القصاص والضمان لارتكابه النهي باكتسابه الفعل الذي أجرى الله تعالى العادة بتخليقه الموت عقبه لا على ما وجد في المحل من المعنى المنافي للحياة على ما مر في مسألة التولد يحققه أن عندهم كان من الجائز أن يكون هذا أجله إن لم يكن واجبًا والضمان لا يجب مع الشك ولا القصاص ومع وجب هاهنا دل أنه إنما وجب ذلك لما ذكرنا ثم الحقيقة أنه لا خلاف في هذه المسألة لأنهم يقولون إن معلوم الله تعالى في انتهاء عمره غير هذا إذ لا يظن هذا بمن خالف هشام بن عمرو من تجويز كون المعدوم معلومًا وإنما يقولون إن الله تعالى يعلم أنه يقتل لا محالة ويعلم أنه لو لم يقتل لعاش إلى وقت كذا على الأصل أنه تعالى يعلم ما يكون ويعلم أن ما لا يكون لو كان كيف كان يكون على ما قال في حق الكفار {وَلَوْ رُدُّوا لَعَادُوا لِمَا نُهُوا عَنْهُ} وإن كان يعلم أنهم لا يردون ونحن ننكر هذا وإنما ننكر كون ذلك مشكوكًا مجهولًا عنده كما يكون ممن يجهل العواقب وهم أيضا يأبون هذا والله الموفق
From among them is al-Farra', and they say: If he had died at his term, then retaliation would not be obligatory, nor would compensation be obligatory when there is a sheep and he slaughtered it because he did good to it. The answer: That the obligation of retaliation and compensation is due to his committing the prohibited [act] by his acquisition of the action which God Most High customarily creates death following, not based on what is found in the locus of the meaning contrary to life, as has passed in the issue of generated acts. This is verified by the fact that according to them, it is possible that this is his term if it were not obligatory, and compensation is not obligatory with doubt nor retaliation. And since it became obligatory here, it indicates that it is obligatory due to what we mentioned. Then the truth is that there is no disagreement in this issue because they say: God's knowledge regarding the end of his lifespan is other than this, for one would not suppose this of those who opposed Hisham ibn 'Amr regarding deeming it possible that non-existent is known. Rather they say: God knows that he will surely be killed, and He knows that if he were not killed he would have lived until a certain time, based on the principle that He knows what will be, and He knows what would be if what is not were to happen, as He said regarding the disbelievers: {And if they were returned, they would return to what they were forbidden} even though He knows they will not be returned. And we deny this, but we only deny that it is doubtful or unknown to Him, as is the case with one who is ignorant of outcomes. And they also reject this. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 952

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The Mu'tazila (specifically al-Farra') object that if the victim died at his appointed term (ajal), then killing him should not incur liability such as retaliation (qisas) or compensation (daman), using an example of slaughtering a sheep as a favor. Nasafi answers that liability is for violating God's prohibition by acquiring the act which God customarily creates death after, not because the death occurred at a wrong time. He then clarifies the real issue: both sides agree that God knows what would have happened if the killer had not acted; the Mu'tazila say God knows the killer causes death inevitably and knows the alternative lifespan, while the author denies that God is ignorant of the outcome. He concludes that there is no disagreement, as both affirm God's knowledge of all possibilities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The killer changes the appointed term of the victim.
Correction: The passage argues that liability is for violating the prohibition, not for changing the term; God's knowledge encompasses both actual and counterfactual outcomes.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, why is retaliation (qisas) obligatory on the killer?
A) Because he changed the appointed term.
B) Because he violated the prohibition by acquiring the act that God customarily creates death following.
C) Because the victim died before his term.
D) Because the killer intended harm.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi argues that liability arises from the prohibited acquisition of an act that God customarily follows with death, not from altering the appointed term; thus the apparent disagreement dissolves.

?

How does this push you to take full ownership of your choices?
?

Definition of Provision and the Mu'tazili Dispute
Nasafi defines rizq linguistically and argues it applies to both property and food.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi definition of rizq and why it includes both lawful and unlawful food, contrary to the Mu'tazili view.

Core Idea
Rizq is not just property; it is whatever sustains you, even if unlawful.

This passage tackles a key theological dispute about provision (rizq). The Mu'tazila argued that unlawful things cannot be provision from Allah, but Imam Nasafi defends the Maturidi position by analyzing the linguistic and Qur'anic evidence. We will see how the meaning of rizq as 'nutrition' resolves the issue.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two positions
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Identify the Maturidi view (everyone eats his own provision, lawful or unlawful) and the Mu'tazili objection (possible not to eat one's own provision and eat another's).
Notice the strong language: 'it is inconceivable' (لا يتصور) for the Maturidi position.
2
Analyze the linguistic definition
Nasafi gives the lexical meaning of rizq as 'measured sustenance' and notes it can refer to absolute ownership (milk) or nutrition (ghidha'). Reflect on why the word is also used for a soldier's stipend (murtaziq).
The dual meaning is crucial for the argument.
3
Examine the Qur'anic evidence
Read the verse from Hud (11:6) quoted by Nasafi. He argues that since animals (dawabb) have no ownership, the verse must refer to nutrition, not property. Consider why this supports the Maturidi position.
If rizq were only property, then haram would not be rizq; but the verse shows it is about food.
4
Understand the conclusion
Nasafi concludes that rizq applies to both ownership and nutrition, and it is impossible to restrict it to ownership alone. Thus, unlawful food is still rizq because Allah creates the nourishment directly.

الكلام في الأرزاق: ثم عندنا كل يستوفى رزق نفسه ويأكله حلالاً كان أو حرامًا ولا يتصور أن لا يأكل إنسان ما جعل رزقا له ولا أن يأكل غيره رزقه أو يأكل هو رزق غيره وقالت المعتزلة من الجائز أن لا يأكل رزقه ويأكل رزق غيره ويأكل رزقه غيره والرزق في اللغة اسم للقوت المقدر ولهذا يسمى من يجري عليه السلطان من المرابطين في كل شهر مقدار مرتزقة وقد يستعمل ويراد به الملك المطلق وقد يستعمل ويراد به الغذاء قال الله تعالى {وَمَا مِن دَابَّةٍ فِي الْأَرْضِ إِلَّا عَلَى اللهِ رِزْقُهَا} والدواب لا ملك لها بالأسباب المشروعة فكان المراد منه ما يحصل لها به الاغتذاء فإن حمل على الملك لم يكن الحرام رزقا والإنسان قد يأكل رزق غيره أي ملكه ويأكل غيره رزقه أي ملكه وإن حمل على الغذاء كان الحرام رزقًا لأن الله تعالى يغذينا أي يخلق التغذي والنمو في أبداننا وهو تعالى يخلقه متفرد لا صنع للعبد فيه ومن المحال إطلاق اسم الرزق على الملك خاصة دون الغذاء بل هو يقع عليها جميعًا ثم
Discourse on provisions: Then according to us, everyone receives his own provision and eats it, whether lawful or unlawful, and it is inconceivable that a person does not eat what has been made a provision for him, nor that another eats his provision, nor that he eats another's provision. The Mu'tazila said: It is possible that one does not eat his provision and eats another's provision, and another eats his provision. Provision (al-rizq) in language is a name for the measured sustenance, and for this reason he upon whom the ruler provides an amount every month from among the garrison soldiers is called a mercenary (murtaziq). It may be used and intended to mean absolute ownership (al-milk al-mutlaq), and it may be used and intended to mean nutrition (al-ghidha'). God Most High said: {And there is no creature on earth except that upon God is its provision}. And creatures (al-dawabb) have no ownership through legal means, so what is intended by it is what obtains nutrition for them. For if it is interpreted as ownership, then the unlawful (al-haram) is not provision, and a person may eat another's provision i.e., his property, and another may eat his provision i.e., his property. And if it is interpreted as nutrition, then the unlawful is provision, because God Most High nourishes us, i.e., He creates nutrition and growth in our bodies, and He creates it alone with no act of the servant in it. And it is impossible to restrict the name 'provision' to ownership alone without nutrition; rather it applies to both. Then
Source pages p. 953

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'rizq' (provision) and presents the Maturidi position that everyone receives and consumes his own provision, whether lawful or unlawful, followed by the Mu'tazili objection that it is possible not to eat one's own provision and eat another's. The author then analyzes the linguistic meaning: rizq in language means measured sustenance, as seen in the term 'murtaziq' for a soldier receiving a stipend. It can refer to absolute ownership (milk) or to nutrition (ghidha'). Using the Qur'anic verse about creatures (dawabb) having no ownership, the author argues that the intended meaning is nutrition, since animals have no property. Thus, if rizq is understood as property, the unlawful (haram) cannot be rizq, but if understood as food, then haram can be rizq because God creates nutrition directly. The author concludes that rizq cannot be restricted to ownership alone; it applies to both meanings.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'rizq' only means property, so haram cannot be provision from Allah.
Correction: Rizq linguistically includes both property and nutrition; the Qur'anic verse about animals shows it refers to food, so haram can be rizq because Allah creates nutrition directly.

Key Takeaway
Rizq is a broad term that encompasses both property and food; the Maturidi position holds that everyone receives their allotted provision, whether lawful or unlawful, because Allah creates nutrition directly.

The Definition of Rizq and the Verbal Dispute
Al-Nasafi defines rizq and resolves a sectarian dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi definition of rizq and why the dispute with the Mu'tazila is considered merely verbal.

Core Idea
Rizq has two meanings; the dispute dissolves when the term is clarified.

In this passage, al-Nasafi tackles a dispute with the Mu'tazila over the nature of provision (rizq). The core question is whether a person can consume something that is not his own provision and whether forbidden food counts as provision. Al-Nasafi begins by distinguishing two meanings of rizq—ownership and food—and then uses Qur'anic verses to show that the dispute is merely linguistic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the full Arabic passage and its English translation. This will give you an overview of the argument before diving into details.
2
Identify the two positions
Notice how al-Nasafi states the Maturidi position ('according to us, everyone consumes his own provision') and contrasts it with the Mu'tazila view that it is possible to eat another's provision.
3
Understand the two meanings of rizq
Al-Nasafi explains that rizq in Arabic has two senses: absolute ownership (milk) and food/nourishment (ghidhā'). He supports this with the Qur'anic verse about creatures.
4
Analyze the Qur'anic evidence
The passage presents two arguments from the Qur'an to prove that forbidden food is not rizq in the sense of ownership. Follow each argument carefully.
5
Grasp the verbal nature of the dispute
Finally, al-Nasafi quotes two scholars who concluded that the disagreement is only in words, not in reality. This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يماثل هذه المسألة مسألة الأرزاق ثم عندنا كل يستوفى رزق نفسه ويأكله حلالاً كان أو حرامًا ولا يتصور أن لا يأكل إنسان ما جعل رزقا له ولا أن يأكل غيره رزقه أو يأكل هو رزق غيره، وقالت المعتزلة: من الجائز أن لا يأكل رزقه ويأكل رزق غيره ويأكل رزقه غيره، والرزق في اللغة اسم للقوت المقدر، ولهذا يسمى من يجري عليه السلطان من المرابطين في كل شهر مقدار مرتزقة، وقد يستعمل ويراد به الملك المطلق، وقد يستعمل ويراد به الغذاء. قال الله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَا مِن دَابَّةٍ فِي الْأَرْضِ إِلَّا عَلَى اللهِ رِزْقُهَا ﴾ والدواب لا ملك لها بالأسباب المشروعة، فكان المراد منه ما يحصل لها به الاغتذاء فإن حمل على الملك لم يكن الحرام رزقا والإنسان قد يأكل رزق غيره أي ملكه ويأكل غيره رزقه أي ملكه، وإن حمل على الغذاء كان الحرام رزقًا لأن الله تعالى يغذينا أي يخلق التغذي والنمو في أبداننا، وهو تعالى يخلقه متفرد لا صنع للعبد فيه، ومن المحال إطلاق اسم الرزق على الملك خاصة دون الغذاء بل هو يقع عليها جميعًا ثم المحرم تناوله لا يجوز أن يكون رزقًا للظالم يدل على ذلك قول الله تعالى: (أَنفِقُوا مِمَّا رَزَقْنَكُم) فلو كان المحرم رزقا لمن تناوله لكان مأمورًا بالإنفاق منه إما على جهة الندب أو الإباجة أو الوجوب، وفي علمنا بأنه منهي عن ذلك دلالة ما قلناه. وقوله تعالى: ﴿ الَّذِينَ يُؤْمِنُونَ بِالْغَيْبِ وَيُقِيمُونَ الصَّلَوَةَ وَمَا رَزَقْهُمْ يُنْفِقُونَ ﴾ يدل على ذلك من وجهين: أحدهما أنهم مدحوا بأنهم ينفقون مما رزقوا فلو كان الحرام رزقا لكان ممدوحا بالإنفاق منه عل بعض الوجوه وفي كونه مذموماً على ذلك دلالة على أنه ليس برزق له. والثاني: أنه تعالى قال: وَمَا رَزَقْهُمْ يُنْفِقُونَ فلولا أن هناك شيء ينفق لم يرزقوه لم يكن لهذا القول معنى، وهذا يبين أن المنفق من المحرمات لم يكن منفقا مما رزقه الله وقد جعل الله تعالى الإنفاق مما رزقه المؤمن من صفات المؤمنين. راجع المغني لعبد الجبار جـ 11 ص 35. ويقبح أن يقال فلان عاش مائة سنة لم يأكل رزق الله تعالى، والشيخ أبو الحسن الرستغفني وأبو إسحاق الأسفرائيني مما حققا الخلاف في مسألة الآجال والأرزاق، وقالا : فيهما خلاف من حيث العبارة لا غير، وهو الصواب.
And similar to this question is the question of provisions (al-arzāq). Then according to us, everyone consumes his own provision and eats it, whether lawful or unlawful, and it is inconceivable that a person does not eat what has been made a provision for him, nor that another eats his provision, nor that he eats another's provision. The Mu'tazila said: It is possible that he does not eat his provision and eats another's provision, and another eats his provision. And provision (al-rizq) in language is a name for the measured sustenance (al-qūt al-muqaddar). For this reason, one upon whom the ruler dispenses from the frontier guards every month a fixed amount is called a 'murtaziq'. And it may be used meaning absolute ownership (al-milk al-muṭlaq), and it may be used meaning food (al-ghidhā'). Allah Most High said: {And there is no creature on earth but that upon Allah is its provision} and the creatures have no ownership by lawful means, so what is intended by it is what enables them to obtain nourishment. If it is interpreted as ownership, then the unlawful is not provision, and a person may eat another's provision, i.e., his ownership, and another eats his provision, i.e., his ownership. And if it is interpreted as food, then the unlawful is provision, because Allah Most High nourishes us, i.e., He creates nourishment and growth in our bodies, and He Most High creates it alone, no act of the servant in it. And it is impossible to apply the name provision specifically to ownership to the exclusion of food, rather it applies to both. Then what is forbidden to consume cannot be a provision for the wrongdoer. Evidence for that is the saying of Allah Most High: 'Spend from that which We have provided you.' For if the forbidden were a provision for the one who consumes it, he would be commanded to spend from it, either by way of recommendation, permission, or obligation. And our knowledge that he is prohibited from that indicates what we said. And His saying Most High: {Those who believe in the unseen and establish prayer and spend from what We have provided them} indicates that in two ways: First, they are praised for spending from what they were provided, so if the forbidden were a provision, they would be praised for spending from it in some respects, and the fact that they are condemned for that indicates that it is not a provision for him. Second, He Most High said: 'and spend from what We have provided them,' so if there were not something they spend which they were not provided, this statement would have no meaning. And this clarifies that the one who spends from forbidden things is not spending from what Allah has provided him, and Allah Most High has made spending from what He provides the believer a characteristic of the believers. Refer to al-Mughnī by 'Abd al-Jabbār, vol. 11, p. 35. And it is repugnant to say: So-and-so lived a hundred years without eating Allah's provision. And Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Rustughfani and Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini thoroughly investigated the disagreement in the question of terms and provisions, and they said: In them there is a disagreement only in terms, not in reality, and that is correct.
Source pages p. 953

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the nature of provision (rizq). Al-Nasafi begins by stating that the issue of provisions is similar to a previously discussed question (probably about lifespan). He then presents the Maturidi position: every person consumes his own provision, whether lawful or unlawful, and it is impossible for a person not to eat what is decreed as his provision, or for someone else to eat it. The Mu'tazila, by contrast, hold that it is possible for a person not to eat his own provision and to eat another's, and for another to eat his. 

Al-Nasafi then turns to the linguistic definition of 'rizq'. In Arabic, it originally means 'measured sustenance' (al-qūt al-muqaddar), hence a soldier who receives a fixed monthly stipend from the ruler is called a 'murtaziq'. However, the term can be used in two senses: (1) absolute ownership (milk), and (2) food/nourishment (ghidhā'). He supports this by quoting the Qur'anic verse: 'And there is no creature on earth but that upon Allah is its provision' (Hud 11:6). He notes that creatures (dawābb) do not have ownership through lawful means, so the verse must refer to the sense of food. If 'rizq' meant only ownership, then the unlawful could not be called provision, and it would be permissible for a person to eat another's ownership. But if it means food, then the unlawful is also provision because Allah creates nourishment in bodies directly, without human agency. Therefore, he concludes that 'rizq' applies to both meanings, and it is impossible to restrict it to ownership alone.

He then provides further evidence using Quranic commands: Allah commands spending from what He has provided (anfiqū mimmā razaqnākum). If the unlawful were provision, then one would be commanded to spend from it, but we know that spending from the unlawful is prohibited, so the unlawful is not provision in the sense of ownership. Similarly, the verse describing believers as spending from what Allah has provided them (mā razaqahum yunfiqūn) indicates that if the unlawful were provision, they would be praised for spending from it, but they are condemned. Therefore, the 'rizq' that is spent must be lawful.

Finally, Al-Nasafi notes that it is repugnant to say someone lived without eating Allah's provision, implying that all food is ultimately from Allah. He then cites two scholars, Abu al-Hasan al-Rustughfani and Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, who concluded that the dispute between the Maturidis/Ash'aris and the Mu'tazila on both lifespan and provision is merely verbal, not substantive. Al-Nasafi affirms that this is correct.

Key Terms
رزق (rizq)
قوت (qūt)
ملك (milk)
غذاء (ghidhā')
معتزلة (Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that forbidden food is absolutely not rizq from Allah.
Correction: Al-Nasafi clarifies that rizq has two senses: as ownership, the unlawful is not rizq; as food, it is rizq from Allah. The dispute is verbal.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what does the term 'murtaziq' refer to?
A) A person who eats forbidden food
B) A frontier guard who receives a fixed monthly stipend
C) A person who disobeys Allah
D) A person who does not eat his own provision

Key Takeaway
The dispute over rizq is resolved by distinguishing its two meanings: as ownership, the forbidden is not provision; as food, it is, making the disagreement merely verbal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consume something that is doubtful or forbidden, do you acknowledge that the nourishment itself comes from Allah, yet also recognize that it is not what He willed for you to own? How does this awareness change your attitude towards your earnings and consumption?
?
The lesson shows that the dispute about rizq is merely verbal, yet many Muslims today argue fiercely over definitions. Where in your own life have you prioritized linguistic victories over the reality of faith and action?
?
If you were to truly embrace that everything you eat is from Allah's provision in the sense of nourishment, how would that transform your gratitude and contentment, even when you face scarcity or the unlawful appears abundant?

Disagreement on Ages and Provisions is Verbal
Nasafi reports that disagreement on ages and provisions is merely verbal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the apparent disagreement about whether someone can live without eating God's provision is actually a verbal dispute, not a doctrinal one.

Core Idea
Differences about provision are only in words, not in belief.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi addresses a debate about the scope of God's provision. Some might think that saying someone lived a long time without eating God's provision implies a denial of divine sustenance. Nasafi reports that two leading scholars, al-Rustughfani and al-Isfara'ini, investigated this and concluded that any difference here is merely verbal — not a real theological disagreement. This lesson explains their reasoning and why it matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the key phrase 'خلاف من حيث العبارة لا غير' which means 'disagreement only in expression'.
The repetition at the end ('سبق التعريف به') is likely a marginal note indicating these terms were defined earlier.
2
Identify the scholars and their conclusion
Find the names of the two scholars (al-Rustughfani and al-Isfara'ini) and note their shared conclusion: the disagreement is only verbal.
3
Understand why the statement is 'ugly'
Reflect on why Imam Nasafi says 'يقبح' (it is ugly) to say someone lived 100 years without eating God's provision. How does this relate to the doctrine of God's universal provision?
The statement is considered repugnant because it seems to deny that God provides for all.
4
Apply the lesson
Consider how this distinction between verbal and substantive disagreement can be applied to other theological debates.
This shows that not all differences of opinion affect core doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقبح أن يقال فلان عاش مائة سنة لم يأكل رزق الله تعالى، والشيخ أبو الحسن الرستغفني (۱) وأبو إسحاق الأسفرائيني (٢) مما حققا الخلاف في مسألة الآجال والأرزاق، وقالا : فيهما خلاف من حيث العبارة لا غير، وهو الصواب(۳). سبق التعريف به. سبق التعريف به.
It is ugly to say that so-and-so lived one hundred years without eating the provision of God Almighty. And Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Rustughfani (1) and Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini (2) thoroughly investigated the disagreement in the matter of terms (ajal) and provisions (arzaq), and they said: In them (the matters) there is disagreement only in terms of expression, nothing else, and that is the correct (view). It has been previously defined. It has been previously defined.
Source pages p. 954

Commentary
This passage is a brief report and judgment from Imam Nasafi on a specific theological-linguistic debate about God's provision. It begins by stating that it is considered reprehensible (يقبح) to say that someone lived a long life without eating God's provision, because such a statement implicitly denies that every created being's sustenance comes from God. Nasafi then notes that two leading scholars, Abu al-Hasan al-Rustughfani and Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini, investigated the disagreement concerning the matters of 'terms' (al-ajal, lifespans) and 'provisions' (al-arzaq) — two linked topics in kalam about divine decree. Their conclusion was that any difference of opinion in these matters is merely verbal (خلاف من حيث العبارة لا غير), and this conclusion is the correct one (وهو الصواب). In other words, when people argue about whether someone can be said not to have consumed God's provision, the real disagreement is not about the underlying doctrine of God's universal provision, but about the appropriate phrasing. The repetition of 'سبق التعريف به' (it has been previously defined) may be a marginal annotation indicating that the definitions of these terms have been given earlier in the work, or it may be a scribal note.

Key Terms
آجال (ajal)
أرزاق (arzaq)
خلاف لفظي (verbal disagreement)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that there is a real theological disagreement about whether God provides for all, and that saying someone lived without eating God's provision is acceptable.
Correction: The passage clarifies that any disagreement is only about wording; the underlying doctrine that God provides for all is not disputed.

D) It concerns only provisions, not ages.

Key Takeaway
The disagreement about ages and provisions is only verbal; the correct view is that it is a matter of expression, not doctrine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Divine Decree

Divine Will: Knowledge vs Command
Nasafi presents the Maturidi simplification and contrasts it with the Mu'tazili view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi and Mu'tazili views on the basis of divine will, and how they differ in relating will to knowledge or command.

Core Idea
Divine will agrees with knowledge in Maturidi theology, but with command in Mu'tazili theology.

This lesson presents a sharp contrast between two major theological schools on the relationship between God's will, His knowledge, and His commands. The Maturidi position is a pedagogical simplification: God wills whatever He knows will occur, whether good or evil. The Mu'tazila, however, hold that God wills what He commands and detests what He forbids, regardless of what He knows will happen. By studying these two views side by side, you will grasp the central axis of disagreement on divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the English translation. Notice the parallel structure of the two views.
The passage uses the examples of Pharaoh and Abu Jahl to illustrate both positions.
2
Identify the Maturidi position
Find the sentence that states the Maturidi view: God wills the existence of what He knows will exist, and wills the non-existence of what He knows will not exist. Note that this applies to all things, regardless of moral value.
The phrase 'what Allah knew would exist, He willed its existence' is key.
3
Identify the Mu'tazili position
Find the sentence that states the Mu'tazili view: God wills the existence of what He commands, even if He knows it will not occur; and He detests and wills the non-existence of what He forbids, even if He knows it will occur.
Contrast this with the previous step.
4
Understand the axis of disagreement
The passage concludes: 'Thus the will of Allah, with us, is in accordance with knowledge, and with them, in accordance with command, and upon this revolves the disagreement.' This is the core difference.
Memorize this sentence as the summary of the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومشايخنا - رحمهم الله - يقولون تيسيراً على المتعلمين بأن ما علم الله أن يوجد أراد وجوده شرًا كان أو خيرًا ، قبيحًا كان أو حسنًا، طاعة كان أو معصية (وما علم أنه لا يكون أراد أن لا يكون شرًا كان أو خيرًا، قبيحا كان أو حسنًا، طاعة كان أو معصية) ، فالله تعالى لما علم أنه يوجد من فرعون وأبي جهل وغيرهما من الكفرة الكفر ، أراد منهم الكفر وإن نهاهم عنه، ولما علم أن لا يوجد منهم الإيمان أراد أن لا يوجد منهم الإيمان وإن أمرهم به. وزعمت المعتزلة أن ما أمر الله تعالى به أراد وجوده، وإن علم أنه لا يوجد وما نهى عنه كره وجوده، وأراد أن لا يوجد وإن علم وجوده فلما كان أمر فرعون بالإيمان أراد منه الإيمان وإن علم أنه لا يوجد منه، ولما نهاه عن الكفر أراد أن لا يوجد منه الكفر وإن علم أنه يوجد فكانت إرادة الله عندنا موافقة للعلم وعندهم موافقة للأمر، وعلى هذا يدور الخلاف.
And our shaykhs - may Allah have mercy on them - say, as a facilitation for learners, that what Allah knew would exist, He willed its existence, whether evil or good, ugly or beautiful, obedience or disobedience; and what He knew would not exist, He willed that it not exist, whether evil or good, ugly or beautiful, obedience or disobedience. So Allah most high, since He knew that from Pharaoh and Abu Jahl and other disbelievers, disbelief would exist, He willed from them disbelief even though He forbade them from it; and since He knew that from them faith would not exist, He willed that faith should not exist from them even though He commanded them with it. And the Mu'tazila claimed that what Allah most high commanded, He willed its existence, even if He knew it would not exist; and what He forbade, He detested its existence and willed that it not exist, even if He knew it would exist. So when He commanded Pharaoh with faith, He willed from him faith even though He knew it would not occur from him; and when He forbade him from disbelief, He willed that disbelief should not exist from him even though He knew it would exist. Thus the will of Allah, with us, is in accordance with knowledge, and with them, in accordance with command, and upon this revolves the disagreement.
Source pages p. 956

Commentary
This passage presents two contrasted doctrines. Nasafi first reports the pedagogical simplification (taysīr) taught by Maturidi scholars: God wills the existence of whatever He knows will occur, regardless of its moral quality (good or evil, obedience or disobedience), and He wills the non-existence of whatever He knows will not occur. This is illustrated by the example of Pharaoh and Abu Jahl: Allah knew they would persist in disbelief, so He willed that disbelief from them, even though He forbade them from it; and He knew they would not believe, so He willed that they not believe, even though He commanded them to believe. Then Nasafi reports the Mu'tazili view: God wills the existence of what He commands (whether it actually occurs or not) and detests and wills the non-existence of what He forbids (whether it actually occurs or not). The same example: since Allah commanded Pharaoh to believe, He willed faith from him, even though He knew it would not happen; and since He forbade disbelief, He willed that it not occur, even though He knew it would. The key difference: for Maturidis, divine will aligns with divine knowledge (ʿilm); for Mu'tazila, divine will aligns with divine command (amr). This is the axis of the doctrinal dispute.

Key Terms
علم
إرادة
أمر
نهي
وجود
عدم
مشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidi view implies that God desires or loves evil and disobedience.
Correction: The passage shows that God wills the existence of evil only in the sense of allowing it consistent with His knowledge, not that He approves or loves it. The previous lesson (if included) clarifies that God does not command or love evil.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi pedagogical simplification, what does Allah will regarding something He knows will not occur?
A) He wills its existence because He commands it.
B) He wills that it not exist.
C) He neither wills nor detests it.
D) He wills it only if it is good.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi view that divine will conforms to knowledge contrasts with the Mu'tazili view that it conforms to command, forming the central disagreement on this topic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah's will is based on His perfect knowledge, even when He wills things you find painful or confusing? How does this shape your response to trials?
?
The Mu'tazila insisted God's will must match His commands. Have you ever felt that Allah's decree should be different because it doesn't match what you think is good? What does this lesson teach you about submitting to His will?
?
Think of a situation where you desired something Allah forbade. How does knowing that Allah's knowledge is perfect and His will is based on that knowledge help you align your desires with His commands?

Refuting the Necessary Link Between Bayan and Sharh al-Sadr
Refutes the claim that bayan always causes sharh al-sadr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that divine explanation (bayan) always produces spiritual openness (sharh al-sadr) leads to an impossible contradiction and invalidates God's division of creation.

Core Idea
If bayan caused sharh al-sadr, disbelievers would have both opened and constricted chests.

This passage tackles a key theological claim: that God's explanation (bayan) automatically grants a person spiritual openness (sharh al-sadr). The author, Imam al-Nasafi, demonstrates the absurd consequences of this view. By showing that it would force disbelievers to have contradictory states and render God's classification meaningless, he refutes the claim decisively.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic source and its English translation side by side. Notice the logical structure: 'if...then...' followed by two impossible outcomes.
The passage is dense but tightly argued.
2
Identify the refuted claim
The claim being refuted is that sharh al-sadr always occurs when bayan occurs. Put this claim in your own words before moving on.
3
Understand the first impossible consequence
The passage argues that if the claim were true, then every disbeliever who receives bayan would have an opened chest. But since God calls them misguided, they would also have a constricted chest. A person cannot have both simultaneously—this is a contradiction.
This is the 'impossible' mentioned in the text.
4
Understand the second impossible consequence
The second consequence is that God's division of people into those with opened chests and those with constricted chests would be invalid. If everyone has an opened chest, then no one remains without it, making the division pointless. Attributing such an error to God is disbelief.
This refutes the claim from the perspective of divine wisdom.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with a strong statement: attributing error to God is kufr. The author seeks protection from such falsehood. Consider how this refutation strengthens the Maturidi position on human responsibility and divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن شرح الصدر لو كان يقع للبيان والبيان واقع للكل، لكان كل من وقع له البيان وقع له شرح الصدر، فكان كل كافر مشروح الصدر لحصول البيان له، ولكان يقع له ولكان يقع ضيق الصدر لتسميته تعالى إياه ضالاً، فكان كل كافر مشروح الصدر لحصول البيان له، ضيق الصدر لتسميته تعالى إياه ضالاً وهو محال، وكذا قسمة الله تعالى الخلق إلى من شرح له الصدر، وإلى من جعل صدره ضيقًا باطلة إذ كل لما كان البيان واقعا وكان شرح الصدر له حاصلاً لم يبق أحد ليس بمشروح الصدر البتة، ونسبة الله تعالى إلى الخطأ فى القسمة كفر وضلال، وبالله العصمة.
Because if the opening of the breast (sharh al-sadr) occurred due to explanation (al-bayan) and explanation occurs for everyone, then everyone to whom explanation occurs would have opening of the breast. Therefore every disbeliever would have an opened breast due to explanation occurring for him, and he would also have constriction of the breast due to God Almighty calling him misguided. So every disbeliever would have an opened breast due to explanation occurring for him, and constriction of the breast due to God Almighty calling him misguided — and this is impossible. Likewise, God Almighty's division of creation into those whose breast He opened and those whose breast He made constricted would be invalid, since when explanation occurs for everyone and opening of the breast obtains for everyone, no one at all would remain without an opened breast. Attributing error in division to God Almighty is disbelief and misguidance. And with God is protection.
Source pages p. 963

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument — likely from the Mu'tazila — that God's explanation (bayan) always produces spiritual openness (sharh al-sadr). The author, Imam al-Nasafi, shows the absurd consequences of this claim. First, if explanation always causes sharh al-sadr, then every disbeliever who has received explanation would have an opened breast. But at the same time, God calls them misguided, implying constriction. Thus a disbeliever would simultaneously have both an opened and constricted breast, which is impossible. Second, God's classification of people into those with opened breasts and those with constricted breasts would be pointless, because if all have opened breasts, no one remains without it. Attributing such an erroneous classification to God is disbelief. The author concludes by seeking refuge in God from such falsehood.

Correction: The passage shows this leads to absurdity: disbelievers would have contradictory states, and God's division would be pointless. Bayan alone does not guarantee sharh al-sadr; human response matters.

Key Takeaway

?

Will you seek God to open your heart, or remain satisfied with mere intellectual assent?
?

?
If God's exposition alone does not guarantee openness, then your own effort in seeking openness is insufficient; it depends on God's decree. Are you living with that dependence, or do you assume that listening to more lectures or reading more books will automatically soften your heart? The Meaning of God's Will to Misguide: A Dialectical Argument and Refutation
Dialectical argument on the meaning of 'yudillu' and refutation of Mu'tazila interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi theologians argued that God's will to misguide refers to His naming someone misguided, and how they refuted Mu'tazila interpretations through logical dilemmas and Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
God's will to misguide is about naming, not creating misguidance.

This passage presents a nuanced theological argument about the nature of divine will in relation to misguidance. Through logical questioning and scriptural analysis, the author defends the position that God's will encompasses all things, including the naming of someone as misguided, while refuting Mu'tazila interpretations that limit divine will. We will follow the dialectical reasoning and examine the Quranic evidence from the story of Noah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get the rhythm. Then read the English translation. Notice the dialectical style—questions posed to opponents.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Follow the dialectical argument on 'yudillu'
The author starts with a definition: 'wills to mislead' means 'wills to call him misguided.' Then a dilemma is posed: does God will actual misguidance? The two possible answers lead to contradictions.
Map out the if-then logic on paper.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence from Noah's verse
The author cites Quran 11:34 and argues that the Mu'tazila interpretation is invalid because it would imply Noah's advice always profits, which contradicts the story.
Consider the conditional structure of the verse.
4
Reflect on the refutation of Ja'far ibn Harb
The author rejects Ja'far's claim that the verse was directed at 'Jabariyya' among Noah's people. Note that the argument cuts off; the refutation is incomplete.
This part is cut off in the source; we have only the beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لهم : لما كان معنى قوله : ( وَمَن يُرِدان يُضِلَّهُ ) أي من يرد أن يسميه ضالاً كانت تسميته ضالاً داخله تحت الإرادة، فبعد ذلك نقول: إذا أراد أن يسميه ضالاً، هل يريد ضلالته؟ فإن قالوا: نعم، تركوا مذهبهم وانقادوا للحق، وإن قالوا: لا. قيل لهم : إذا أرادوا تسميته ضالاً ولم يرد ضلالة بل أراد اهتداءه، فلو حصل ما أراد وهو الاهتداء ولم يحصل ما لم يرد وهو الضلال، أكان في تسميته ضالاً صادقا أم كاذبًا؟ فإن قالوا: كان صادقًا ظهرت مكابرتهم، وإن قالوا: كان كاذبًا، قيل : كان إذا مريدًا كونه كاذبًا في تسميته ضالاً وهو سفه – جل الله تعالى عن ذلك. وقال تعالى خبرًا عن نوح عليه السلام حيث قال لقومه : ( وَلَا يَنفَعُكُمْ نصحِيَ إِنْ أَرَدتُ أَنْ أَنصَحَ لَكُمْ إِن كَانَ اللهُ يُرِيدُ أَن يُغْوِيَكُمْ ) ذكر أن الله يريد أن يغوي الكفرة، وتأويل المعتزلة أن في الآية أن نصحة لا ينفع إن كان الله يريد أن يغويهم وليس فيه أنه يريد، تأويل فاسد ، لأنه إذا كان يستحيل منه إرادة غوايتهم أي فائدة في ذكر الآية على الأمر ، لو كان كما يزعمون لكان ينبغي أن يكون مراد نوح عليه السلام من هذه الآية بيان أن نصحه ينفعهم لا محالة، لأن فيه تعليق نفي نفع النصح بما يستحيل ثبوته وهو إرادة إغوائهم، وتعليق النفي بما يستحيل ثبوته يكون تأكيدًا للإثبات، ويصير كأنه قال : لا ينفعكم نصحي، إذا كان الله يريد أن يغويكم فإذا استحال أن يريد أن يغويكم ينفعكم نصحي وحيث رأينا أنه كان لا ينفعهم وإنما قال ذلك يأسا من نفع نصحه لهم عند إرادة الله أن يغويهم، فكان ذلك دليلاً على فساد التأويل. وما قاله جعفر بن حرب أن الآية تدل أنه كان في قوم نوح عليه السلام قوم مجبرة يقولون : إن الله تعالى يريد الفساد فخاطبهم منبها لهم على بطلان قولهم فقال: وَلَا يَنفَعُكُمْ نُصْحِي إِنْ أَرَدتُ أَنْ أَنصَحَ لَكُمْ فيما أدعوكم إن كان الأمر كما ذكرتم من أنه تعالى المريد لفسادكم ويخلق الكفر فيكم. فاسد ، لأن الأمر لو كان كما زعم
Then we say to them: Since the meaning of His statement, 'And whoever He wills to mislead' (Quran 4:88?) — meaning whoever He wills to call misguided — then His calling him misguided falls under His will. After that we say: If He wills to call him misguided, does He will his actual misguidance? If they say: Yes, they abandon their doctrine and submit to the truth; and if they say: No. It is said to them: If He wills to call him misguided and does not will misguidance but rather wills his guidance, then if what He wills — guidance — occurs, and what He does not will — misguidance — does not occur, would His calling him misguided be truthful or false? If they say: It is truthful, their obstinacy is exposed; and if they say: It is false, it is said: Then He wills him to be false in calling him misguided, and that is foolishness — God Almighty is exalted above that. And God Almighty related from Noah, peace be upon him, when he said to his people: 'Nor will my advice profit you if I intend to advise you if God intends to lead you astray' (Quran 11:34). He mentioned that God intends to lead the disbelievers astray. The Mu'tazila interpretation is that the verse means his advice does not profit if God intends to lead them astray, and it does not state that He intends [misguidance]. This is an invalid interpretation, because if it were impossible for Him to intend their misguidance, what benefit would there be in mentioning the verse in that matter? If it were as they claim, then it would be necessary that the meaning of Noah, peace be upon him, from this verse was to clarify that his advice necessarily profits them, because it attaches the negation of the benefit of advice to something whose existence is impossible — namely the intention of their misguidance. Attaching negation to something whose existence is impossible is an affirmation of the positive, and it becomes as if he said: 'My advice does not profit you if God intends to lead you astray; but if it is impossible that He intends to lead you astray, then my advice profits you.' Yet we see that it did not profit them, and he only said this out of despair of his advice profiting them when God intended to lead them astray. So that is evidence of the invalidity of the interpretation. And what Ja'far ibn Harb said — that the verse indicates that among Noah's people there were 'Jabariyya' who said that God Almighty intends corruption, so he addressed them, alerting them to the falsehood of their statement, saying: 'Nor will my advice profit you if I intend to advise you...' in what I call you, if the matter is as you have mentioned, that He is the one who intends your corruption and creates disbelief in you — is false, because the matter, if it were as he claimed...
Source pages p. 963

Commentary
This passage is a complex theological argument addressing the meaning of God's will ('iraada) in relation to misguidance. The author first engages in a dialectical exchange with opponents (likely Mu'tazila) about the verse 'Whoever He wills to mislead' (Quran 4:88). He argues that the phrase 'wills to mislead' means 'wills to call him misguided' — i.e., the act of naming, not creating misguidance. He then poses a dilemma: if God wills to call someone misguided, does He will actual misguidance? If the opponent says yes, they contradict their own doctrine; if no, the author presses further: if God only wills the naming and not the actual misguidance, then if the person is guided, is God's calling him misguided truthful or false? If truthful, then the person is actually misguided (contradiction); if false, then God wills falsehood — impossible. The author then cites the verse of Noah (Quran 11:34) as evidence that God does intend misguidance for some. The Mu'tazila interpret the verse as a conditional that does not affirm actual intention. The author refutes this by showing that if God's intention of misguidance were impossible, the verse would affirm the benefit of Noah's advice, but in reality it did not benefit them. Finally, the author refutes Ja'far ibn Harb's interpretation that the verse was directed at 'Jabariyya' among Noah's people, but the text cuts off before completing the refutation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's will to call someone misguided implies He wills actual misguidance.
Correction: The author argues that 'wills to mislead' means 'wills to call him misguided' — a distinction that avoids affirming that God directly creates misguidance in the person.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the phrase 'wills to mislead' (يُضِلَّهُ) in the Quran mean?
A) God creates misguidance in the person.
B) God wills to call the person misguided.
C) God abandons the person.
D) God punishes the person.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi argument holds that God's will to misguide is an act of naming, not of causing misguidance, and the Quranic verses support this view against Mu'tazila rationalizations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The Mu'tazila twisted a clear verse to protect their own notion of God's justice. Where in your own heart do you find yourself resisting or reinterpreting clear teachings of the Quran and Sunnah because they make you uncomfortable about Allah's absolute sovereignty? Will you submit to His words even when they challenge your understanding?

Rational Proof Against Dualism: God's Will Unthwartable
Al-Nasafi presents rational proof that God's will cannot be thwarted, refuting dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Nasafi uses a reductio ad absurdum to refute dualism by showing that if God's will could be thwarted, it would imply weakness in God and validate the dualist position.

Core Idea
If God's will is not always realized, then God is weak and dualism is true—an impossible conclusion.

Al-Nasafi presents a rational argument that builds on Qur'anic verses which state that if God willed, He could make everyone believe. He then shows that the existence of disbelief despite God's will leads to an absurdity: that a created being's will can overcome God's will, implying weakness in God. This refutes the dualist notion of two equal powers.

Guided Path
1
Read the Qur'anic verses
Begin by reading the three cited verses that affirm God's complete power over human belief and disbelief. Notice the conditional 'if He willed'.
These verses establish the premise that God can will faith or guidance for all.
2
Follow the rational argument
Next, read the rational argument carefully. It sets up a hypothetical: if God wills faith from a disbeliever, but the disbeliever wills disbelief, then if disbelief occurs, God's will is thwarted. That would mean the disbeliever's will is more effective than God's.
The argument extends to Iblis and the majority of disbelievers.
3
Understand the king analogy
Al-Nasafi then gives an analogy: a king whose will is not carried out in his kingdom is weak. Applying that to God is impossible. This leads to the conclusion that the dualist proof of mutual prevention (tamānuʿ) is refuted and dualism itself is validated if one accepts the premise.
The argument is a reductio: if you deny God's will is always effective, you end up with dualism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعالى: ﴿ فَلَوْ شَاءَ لَهَدَنكُمْ أَجْمَعِينَ ﴾ وقال تعالى: ﴿ وَلَوْ شَاءَ رَبُّكَ لَآمَنَ مَن فِي الْأَرْضِ كُلُهُمْ جَمِيعًا ﴾ وقال: ﴿ وَلَوْ شَاءَ اللهُ مَا أَشْتَرَكُوا ﴾.
والأدلة العقلية لأهل الحق: والمعقول أنه تعالى لو شاء من الكافر الإيمان والكافر شاء من نفسه الكفر، ثم كان الكفر دون الإيمان لتعطلت مشيئة الله تعالى بمشيئة الكافر، وكانت مشيئة الكافر أنفذ من مشيئة الله تعالى وكذا مشيئة إبليس لعنه الله أنفذ في سلطان الله تعالى من مشيئة الله تعالى لكون أكثر الخلق كافرين، وكذا من أدل الدلائل على ضعف الملك وعجزه أن يوجد في ملكه ما لا يشاء، ويشاء أشياء فلا تكون، ولا يشاء أشياء فتكون على كره منه، ووصف الله تعالى بذلك محال، وهذا يرد على دلالة التمانع بالإبطال ويؤدي إلى تصحيح مذهب الثنوية.
He, exalted, said: 'If He willed, He would have guided you all.' And He, exalted, said: 'And if your Lord willed, everyone on earth would have believed, all of them.' And He said: 'And if God willed, they would not have associated partners.' The rational proofs of the people of truth: The rational is that if the Exalted willed faith from the disbeliever and the disbeliever willed disbelief from himself, then if disbelief occurred instead of faith, God's will would be thwarted by the disbeliever's will, and the disbeliever's will would be more effective than God's will. Similarly, the will of Iblis, curse of God upon him, would be more effective in God's dominion than God's will, since most of creation are disbelievers. Also, among the strongest indications of a king's weakness and incapacity is that something he does not will exists in his kingdom, and he wills things that do not occur, and he does not will things that occur against his will. Describing God with that is impossible. This refutes the proof of mutual prevention (tamānuʿ) and leads to the validation of the dualist sect.
Source pages p. 967

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof (dalīl ʿaqlī) from the Ahl al-Ḥaqq (the people of truth). It begins by citing three Qur'anic verses that affirm that if God willed, He could make all people believers or guided. Then the rational argument is presented: If God willed faith from a disbeliever, and that disbeliever willed disbelief from himself, then if disbelief occurs (as it does), it means the disbeliever's will overcame God's will. This would imply that the disbeliever's will is more effective (anfadh) than God's will, and similarly the will of Iblis would be more effective than God's will since most people are disbelievers. Such a situation would be a sign of weakness and incapacity in a king, and it is impossible to attribute that to God. The argument then concludes that this reasoning refutes the proof of mutual prevention (dalīl al-tamānuʿ) used by dualists and actually supports the dualist position (madhhab al-thanawiyya) if one were to accept that God's will can be thwarted. In other words, the fact that many people are disbelievers despite God's will that they believe (according to the Qur'anic verses) shows that if one denies that God's will is always effective, one ends up with the absurdity of two equal wills, which is the dualist position. The proof is structured as a reductio ad absurdum against those who claim that God's will is not always realized.

Key Terms
المشيئة
الإيمان
الكفر
التمانع
الثنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the argument proves that God actually wills disbelief for some people.
Correction: The argument is a hypothetical reductio; it does not assert that God wills disbelief. It assumes that for the sake of argument, if God willed faith and it did not happen, then God's will would be thwarted—an impossibility.

Checkpoint
What does the rational argument say would be the result if the disbeliever's will overcomes God's will?
A) God's will is still effective.
B) It would imply God is weak and the disbeliever's will is more effective.
C) It would prove the existence of multiple gods.
D) It would show that God does not will anything.

Key Takeaway
God's will is always effective; if it were not, dualism would be logically necessary—but that is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever secretly believed that your own will—your plans, desires, or choices—can override God’s will in your life? How does this proof expose that as a form of hidden dualism, and what concrete step can you take today to surrender a specific plan to His will?
?
The proof shows that if God’s will were not always effective, He would be like a weak king. Is there any area of your life where you treat God’s commands as negotiable, as if His will might not be done? What would it look like to live with total trust in the effectiveness of His will, even when you don’t understand His decree?
?
The analogy of the king forces you to see that if your life contains outcomes you did not will, you are not sovereign. But God is. Are you willing to examine a recent disappointment or blessing and honestly say, ‘This happened because God willed it, and His will is always effective’? How would that change your response to it?

Understanding the Mu'tazila Objection on God's Will
Mu'tazila object that the verses mean God compels faith, and God has power to compel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's objection to the previous argument about God's will, and how they reinterpret Qur'anic verses to defend God's power without affirming compulsion.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue that God's will means He could compel faith, but He does not.

This passage presents the Mu'tazila's counter-argument to the earlier rational proofs. They object that the verses do not prove God's will is sometimes thwarted; rather, they show that God could have forced everyone to believe but chose not to. Their objection forces us to clarify the nature of God's will and power.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the Mu'tazila reinterpret the verses as referring to 'will of compulsion' (mashī'at al-jabr).
2
Identify their key move
The Mu'tazila distinguish between what God can do (compel) and what He actually does. Find the two sentences that state God's power to create faith and repel disbelief by compulsion.
This is the core of their rational argument.
3
Consider the implication
Why does this objection matter? It challenges the idea that God's will can be resisted by human free will. Reflect on how this objection preserves God's power while denying compulsion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاعترضت المعتزلة على هذه الآيات وعلى هذا المعقول، فزعمت أن المراد من المشيئة المذكورة في الآيات مشيئة الجبر أي لو شاء ربك لجبرهم على الهدى ولآمنوا جبرًا وما أشركوا، وبهذا اعترضوا أيضا على المعقول فإنهم يقولون: انعدام ما يشاء أو وجود ما لا يشاء إنما يدل على الضعف أن لو لم تكن له قدرة إيجاد ما يشاء ودفع ما لا يشاء وله قدرة إيجاد إيمان كل كافر جبرًا منه. وقدرة دفع كل كفر جبرًا منه، ومن هذا وصفه لا يوصف بالضعف.
Then the Mu'tazila objected to these verses and to this rational argument, claiming that what is meant by the will mentioned in the verses is the will of compulsion (jabr), i.e., 'If your Lord willed, He would have compelled them to guidance, and they would have believed by compulsion, and they would not have associated partners.' With this they also objected to the rational argument, for they say: The non-occurrence of what He wills or the occurrence of what He does not will only indicates weakness if He did not have the power to bring about what He wills and to repel what He does not will. But He has the power to bring about the faith of every disbeliever by compulsion from Him, and the power to repel every disbelief by compulsion from Him. Because of this, He is not described as weak.
Source pages p. 967

Commentary
This passage presents the objection (iʿtirāḍ) of the Mu'tazila against the previous rational argument. They reinterpret the Qur'anic verses (which say 'if God willed, He would have guided all') by saying that 'will' here means 'will of compulsion' (mashīʾat al-jabr). That is, if God had willed, He would have forced (jabara) them to believe, and they would have believed by force. They then address the rational argument: the fact that some things God wills do not occur (like universal faith) does not prove weakness, because God has the power to create faith in every disbeliever by compulsion and to remove disbelief by compulsion. Since He has this power, He is not weak; He simply does not use it. Thus, the claim that God's will is thwarted by the disbeliever's will is invalid because God could override it if He wished. The Mu'tazila's objection hinges on the distinction between what God actually does and what He has the power to do: they argue that God's not compelling faith does not mean He cannot.

Key Terms
المشيئة
الجبر
الاعتراض
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Mu'tazila are affirming jabr (compulsion) as a real phenomenon.
Correction: The Mu'tazila use jabr as a hypothetical to defend God's power. They do not claim God actually compels; they argue that He could, but does not, to preserve human responsibility.

Checkpoint
How do the Mu'tazila explain the verses that say God wills guidance for all?
A) They mean God wills guidance but humans reject it.
B) They mean God could have compelled them, but He does not.
C) They mean God only wills guidance for believers.
D) They are metaphorical and not about will.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that the verses do not undermine God's power; they show He could compel faith but does not, preserving both His power and human responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God could force you to believe without any effort from you, would you prefer that? Why do you think He leaves the choice and struggle with you?
?
When you reflect on your own faith, are there moments you wish God would just compel you to obey? What does that desire reveal about your trust in His wisdom and your own responsibility?
